Row brews

over NY

shooting

NEW YORK: In a matter of seconds, four white police officers from the elite Street Crime Unit fired 41 shots at an unarmed man, a black West African immigrant who had no police record and spoke with a stutter.

Amadou Diallo, a street peddler described as religious and hard-working, was hit 19 times and died instantly in the vestibule of his apartment building in the Bronx on Friday.

The officers' lawyer said Mr Diallo gestured with his hands, leading the police to think he was reaching for a gun.

But with the officers the only witnesses, Amnesty International and the NAACP are demanding explanations.

US Attorney Mary Jo White said federal prosecutors are reviewing the case for possible civil rights violations.

The plainclothes police who shot Mr Diallo had been looking for a rape-murder suspect when they encountered the 22-year-old immigrant.

"There are some similarities between this individual and the sketch we have," the police commissioner said.

Two of the police fired 16 bullets each, emptying their 9mm, semi-automatic pistols in less than five seconds. The others fired their pistols four and five times.

The New York Post


February 10, 1999, Wednesday


ANGUISH OF COP-SLAY VICTIM'S MOM ; COLLAPSES IN GRIEF AT BX. SCENE


BYLINE: Ikimulisa Sockwell, Frankie Edozien and Tracy Connor


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 003


LENGTH: 553 words


The heartbroken mom of an unarmed immigrant blown away by cops collapsed and cried out for justice yesterday when she saw the bullet-riddled building where he was killed.

"I'm hurt! I'm hurt!" Kadiadou Diallo wailed, her knees buckling beneath her outside the brick rowhouse in The Bronx where her son, Amadou Diallo, lived and died.

"Justice must be done," she said after a 30-minute visit to his apartment in the Soundview section.

Flown to New York from the West African nation of Guinea, the dead man's mother first prayed with friends and family at a Parkchester apartment complex.

"I'm suffering, I'm in deep sorrow," she said as she left for the Wheeler Avenue death scene with a police escort.

Pain was etched on her face and she gasped for air as she slowly stepped from a black van outside the two-story building.

"Amadou, Amadou," Mrs. Diallo sobbed after falling to the ground a few feet from the vestibule.

Friends, who also began to weep, pulled her up and helped her through the doorway where her son was killed in a hail of gunfire last Thursday.

She passed walls pockmarked by the 41 bullets fired by four plainclothes cops who claim they mistakenly thought Diallo was reaching for a gun.

She stepped around a growing mound of flowers left by friends and neighbors shocked that a man with no gun and no criminal record could be shot dead by police in his home.

Inside the apartment, she went into the room where her 24-year-old son would return after long days of peddling hats and gloves on 14th Street in Manhattan.

"The first thing she did when she came into the apartment was to ask where he slept and she lay down on his bed," said the slain man's roommate, Abdul Diallo, who is not related to the family.

"She said she wants to die instead of him," said a family friend who was also with her.

The visit was brief, as were the mother's remarks when she left the building.

Her son was "a good boy, well educated, from a good family," she said quietly. "I'm still crying. I am still in sorrow.

"I want to see justice for my son."

Family lawyer Kyle Watters pleaded for privacy for Mrs. Diallo, saying she needs time and space to mourn her son, who arrived in the United States two years ago.

Her husband, who was on a business trip in Vietnam when he got word of his son's death, is expected to arrive in New York tomorrow.

The family, which is considering an independent autopsy, is planning a traditional Muslim ceremony Friday before bringing Amadou's body home for burial in Guinea.

Mayor Giuliani cancelled his plans to spend tomorrow night and Friday in Texas with Gov. George W. Bush in order to attend the service.

Before and after Mrs. Diallo's visit to her son's home, neighborhood residents continued to stop by to leave bouquets, candles and letters.

"I didn't know him, but this just gives me a headache," said Sharon Brown, who lingered outside the vestibule.

"He was one person. To get shot for no reason ... it's not fair."

The New York Post


February 10, 1999, Wednesday


BRING IN THE FEDS, PROTESTERS DEMAND


BYLINE: Reported by Angela C. Allen, Al Guart, Adam Miller, David Seifman and Murray Weiss. Written by Tracy Connor.


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 002


LENGTH: 531 words


Some 2,000 protesters converged on lower Manhattan yesterday to call for a federal takeover of the probe into the police shooting of a West African immigrant.

"We come to the federal government to say, 'This must stop,'" the Rev. Al Sharpton thundered.

The rally at Federal Plaza came as the FBI monitored the Bronx district attorney's probe of the slaying, going so far as to sit in on interviews with civilian witnesses.

The feds have not officially opened an investigation of the Feb. 4 shooting, and agents have not been allowed to watch interviews of police personnel, sources said.

The demonstrators - the NYPD estimated a crowd of 1,200 but there appeared to be more than 2,000 - called for the feds to formally intervene in the case.

"Now there is much ado about the city showing sympathy to the family. That is appropriate," Sharpton said.

"But we don't just want sympathy, we want justice. We can't have real peace until there's justice."

The four officers who fired 41 bullets at street vendor Amadou Diallo - Sean Carroll, Edward McMellon, Kenneth Boss Jr. and Richard Murphy - have been placed on desk duty.

Many at the 2-hour demonstration demanded the cops be charged swiftly with Amadou's death.

"I loved my nephew very deeply and I will miss him daily," said the slain man's uncle, Mamadou Diallo. "My sadness is great with the knowledge that the four people who shot him are still walking freely on the street."

A Bronx grand jury could take up the case this week.

Steven Worth, the cops' lawyer, said they are willing to testify as long as the feds don't take over.

At the rally, protesters chanted, "no justice, no peace," and accused Mayor Giuliani of turning a blind eye to police brutality.

"These cops wouldn't have done this if they didn't think they could get away with it," said Terry Denson of The Bronx.

"I don't think this is the last time we're going to see this. The police are getting more aggressive, dangerously aggressive."

The demonstrators joined a growing chorus of criticism.

Manhattan Borough President C. Virginia Fields and former Mayor Ed Koch plan to hold a news conference today to demand that the NYPD emphasize civil rights with the "same passion" as crime-fighting.

Koch said he believes cops are entitled to the presumption they acted professionally - unless there are facts to the contrary.

"It is inconceivable that 41 bullets would have to be fired at a guy who it turns out doesn't have a gun, doesn't have a criminal record. And at this point we don't know why they did it," he said.

"At this point, I have drawn a conclusion that the burden is on the cops to show they conducted themselves professionally. Common sense tells me at this point that they did not."

The Black and Hispanic Caucus of the City Council also is planning a news conference on a bill to name a street after cop-chokehold victim Anthony Baez, commemorating all victims of police brutality.

Mayor Giuliani has defended the NYPD as being the "most restrained" big-city police department in the nation.

The New York Post


February 10, 1999, Wednesday


OTHER MOTHERS FEAR THEIR SONS COULD BE NEXT


BYLINE: Douglas Montero


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 003


LENGTH: 525 words


THE woman named Passion was easy to spot in the crowd protesting the shooting death of an unarmed immigrant.

She walked faster, more intensely, than the others.

She was small in stature, but the no-frills placard she carried that read, "Another Isolated Incident," seemed to stand out.

She used both arms to carry the sign as high as she could - as if a young thug had a gun pointed to her back.

"I don't even tear up this sign anymore. I just wait for another incident to occur," said Passion, a 40-year-old Harlem playwright, whose arms remained stuck in place like the Tin Man in "The Wizard of Oz."

"It's happening too much ... and that worries me because I'm a mother of a 19-year-old," she said, her extended arms refusing to flinch as she pushed the sign skyward.

Of the more than 2,000 protesters gathered in Foley Square to denounce the police shooting of 24-year-old Amadou Diallo, hundreds were women - like Passion - who felt compelled to join in.

They were from different neighborhoods and socio-economic backgrounds. But these black mothers seemed to share one common phobia - their children may accidentally get shot by cops.

Diallo was gunned down in a hail of bullets because he didn't turn into an ice cube when four cops screamed, "Freeze."

He was dead by the time the cops realized he wasn't a criminal - and wasn't carrying anything more threatening than a beeper.

Some mothers came from home. Others joined in during their lunch break or running an errand in lower Manhattan. Most are black.

All are scared.

"I'm 64 years old and I'm getting tired of being out here," said Dana, a retired city worker. "But I have to be here because the next one could be my son ... I worry about my son, my nephews - they all fit the profile."

Carol Taylor, 67, of Brooklyn, who was carrying a sign that read, "Oh God Cops Keep Killing Our Children," is angry and fearful.

"We are all scared stiff," she said, raising her voice. "Every time my son goes out the door, I worry."

Is this phobia an aberration or do black and Latino mothers have a legitimate reason to be scared?

Are white cops - who make up the majority of the police force - likely keep their fingers closer to the trigger when approaching minorities?

I asked three cops.

Black Cop: "Most cops defer and use more discretion when dealing with a white perp ... You never hear about a cop shooting a unarmed white man by accident."

White Cop: "It doesn't make a difference, I approach everyone with apprehension and fear, whether they are black, white or Hispanic."

Hispanic Cop: "I don't care what the perp's race is - my main concern is safety."

Police Department records show there were 111 incidents in which cops fired at "offenders" last year. The department said it couldn't provide The Post ethnic breakdowns of the shooters and the citizens being shot at.

Without the stats, it's hard to say for sure if these black and Latino mothers have a legitimate fear.

So I'm left asking the question: Can anyone remember the last time an unarmed white man in this city was shot by cops because he acted in a "threatening manner"?

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


February 10, 1999, Wednesday, FIVE STAR LIFT EDITION


NATION


BYLINE: From News Services


SECTION: NEWS, Pg. A7, NATION BRIEFS COLUMN


LENGTH: 869 words


 
 
Kaczynski plans to seek new trial, law professor says

Theodore Kaczynski, imprisoned as the Unabomber, believes he was unfairly forced to plead guilty last year and plans to seek a new trial, a law professor who has corresponded with him said on Tuesday. Michael Mello of Vermont Law School said in an interview that Kaczynski has objected in letters to the circumstances that led to his guilty plea last January. His lawyers portrayed him as mentally ill, and the judge in the case denied him permission to represent himself. Mello said he had received 58 letters from Kaczynski, who has been in federal prison in Florence, Colo., since he was sentenced to life without the possibility of parole last spring. Kaczynski's desire for a new trial and his correspondence with Mello were described in an article in the Feb. 15 issue of The National Law Journal.
 
Grounded cargo ship leaks oil onto Oregon beaches

Cracks widened in the steel hull of a grounded cargo ship Tuesday, pouring thousands of gallons of oil and diesel fuel onto environmentally sensitive beaches near Coos Bay, Ore. Streaks of tar-like fuel oil streaked the southern Oregon coastline for six miles around the 639-foot New Carissa. As many as 300 workers were called in to mop up the mess with shovels, squeegees and absorbent pompoms.
 
Church leader takes stand in his racketeering trial

Shortly after prosecutors rested their case Tuesday against the Rev. Henry Lyons, the National Baptist Convention USA leader briefly took the witness stand in the Largo, Fla., courtroom. Judge Susan Schaeffer wanted to hear from Lyons about a 1995 letter offering a list of the church organization's purported 8.5 million members to a magazine publisher for $ 50,000. Prosecutors had wanted to admit the letter as evidence in his racketeering trial. When asked by prosecutors whether such a list even existed at that time, he hesitated and then responded: "Just trying to get my dates together. . . . I don't believe so."
 
January's tornadoes more than triple month's record

Last month was a record-setter for tornadoes in the United States. The 163 twisters on record were the most in any January, the National Weather Service said Tuesday. More tornadoes occurred Jan. 21 - 87 - than on any previous January day on record. "Frankly, we consider this to be a significant number of tornadoes for a March day - the height of tornado season. These numbers in January are unprecedented," said Joe Schaefer, director of the National Weather Service's Storm Prediction Center in Norman, Okla. In all, 141 tornadoes struck Arkansas, Louisiana and Tennessee in that outbreak, the agency said. More twisters ripped through six other states earlier in January. The total of 163 is more than three times as many as the previous monthly record for January of 52 tornadoes set in 1975, Schaefer said.
 
Law aims to block Atlanta's suit against gun makers

After heavy lobbying by the National Rifle Association, Georgia Gov. Roy Barnes on Tuesday signed a bill blocking Atlanta's liability lawsuit against gun manufacturers. The law, which takes effect immediately, makes Georgia the first state to prevent a city or county from filing product liability suits against gun makers. The law reserves that right for the state. Barnes, a Democrat, said the state has the right to force Atlanta to avoid the costs of pursuing its lawsuit. Atlanta filed its suit against 17 gun makers last week. As with similar cases in Chicago, New Orleans, Bridgeport, Conn., and Miami, Atlanta accused the industry of negligently designing guns with inadequate safety devices and sought reimbursement for the costs of gun-related violence. The city plans to move forward with its lawsuit and see how the court responds, Mayor Bill Campbell said through a spokesman.
 
Reagan image can't fit on Mount Rushmore, agency says

Former President Ronald Reagan may have an airport and a federal building named after him, but he will not be immortalized on South Dakota's Mount Rushmore, the National Park Service said Tuesday. The agency, which manages the monument, said there is no place within 100 feet on either side of the massive stone faces to carve another image. "It's not about Reagan," but about carving more faces, said spokesman David Barna. The rock on the mountain in South Dakota's Black Hills is too fragile to support more blasting for further carvings, he said. Earlier this month, Rep. Matt Salmon, R-Ariz., said he would introduce legislation to add Reagan's likeness to the shrine alongside other former Presidents G eorge Washington, Thomas Jefferson, Theodore Roosevelt and Abraham Lincoln.
 
Holbrooke will pay ethics fine; nomination moves ahead

President Bill Clinton moved ahead with plans to nominate Richard Holbrooke to be ambassador to the United Nations after Holbrooke agreed Tuesday to pay $ 5,000 to settle civil charges that he violated federal ethics laws. Holbrook, 57, denied the Justice Department's allegations that he improperly contacted a U.S. diplomat on behalf of his employer. Instead, Holbrooke said he was acting as a special government employee asked to explain U.S. policy to Asian officials.

BX.-SLAY COPS BACK ON JOB


BYLINE: Murray Weiss, Bob Hardt, Gregg Birnbaum and Laura Italiano


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 016


LENGTH: 312 words


Three of the four cops implicated in the deadly shooting of an unarmed West African immigrant returned to work yesterday - armed with their guns and badges.

The fourth officer, Sean Carroll - who allegedly fired 16 of the 41 shots - called in sick, a department spokesman said.

The four had been on leave since Thursday, when they opened fire on Amadou Diallo, a street peddler they had come to interview about a serial rape case.

Diallo, 22, a Guinea native, was gunned down in his Wheeler Avenue doorway - struck by 19 of the bullets fired from the officers' guns.

The officers say they opened fire after Amadou disobeyed or misunderstood an order to freeze, and instead reached for his pockets, where he kept only a pager and wallet.

Edward McMellon, 26, Kenneth Boss Jr., 27, Richard Murphy, 26, and Carroll, 35, have been allowed to keep their guns, but they're reassigned to administrative duty.

A Bronx grand jury may begin hearing testimony in the shooting today, to determine if the four will face criminal charges.

Witnesses will include roommates and relatives of Amadou.

It's not clear if the cops will testify.

Yesterday, their lawyer said only that he may let them testify without immunity, providing federal prosecutors not intervene until after The Bronx grand jury completes its deliberations.

Outraged protesters have gathered by the hundreds to decry the brutal shooting, with another rally scheduled for today in front of the federal courthouse in Manhattan.

The shooting was serious enough to attract the attention of federal prosecutors, who are monitoring the Bronx probe.

Yesterday, a spokesman for U.S. Attorney Mary Jo White stressed that she has "complete confidence" in the Bronx effort.

Also yesterday, Mayor Giuliani defended city cops as "the most restrained large city police department in the country."

The New York Post


February 9, 1999, Tuesday


JUST THE FACTS, MA'AM, ON THE FATAL NYPD SHOOTINGS


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 026


LENGTH: 378 words


At moments of high passion, facts are often overtaken by feelings. It's a natural human reaction, but it's no excuse for jumping to politicized conclusions. After four NYPD officers shot and killed Amadou Diallo on Thursday, those who have been arguing that the police department is unnecessarily brutal seemed to have the upper hand, and 1,000 people gathered on Sunday to second that emotion.

But it's simply not borne out by the facts.

In New York, the rate of fatal police shootings - 0.48 per 1,000 cops in 1998 - is the lowest it's been since 1985. That's lower than the rate in Chicago, Dallas, Miami, Philadelphia or Washington, D.C.

Nineteen civilians were killed by the NYPD in 1998, compared with 20 the year before and 30 in 1996.

In fact, police shootings of all kinds have been going down during the Giuliani administration crime crackdown. In 1995, for example, there were 344 police shootings, with an average of five rounds fired per incident. Last year, the incidents dropped to 249, with an average of 3.4 shots fired per incident.

And that's happened even as arrests have026 . 0000.00soared from 309,587 in 1995 to 403,659 in 1998.

What about the Street Crime Unit (SCU), the elite group to which the four officers involved in Thursday's incident belonged? Again the numbers don't support the idea of a bunch of out-of-control cowboys.

In 1997 and 1998, for example, the SCU stopped and frisked a remarkable 45,000 people. It captured 2,072 guns, about 40 percent of all the guns seized citywide. In that time, SCU cops fired their own guns in 28 incidents and killed three people.

All the killings were found to have been justified.

In other words, the overwhelming bulk of NYPD cops who fire their weapons in The Bronx do so only at paper targets at the police department firing range, not at innocent civilians on the street.

This is not to discount the seriousness of the Diallo killing. If the four officers involved in the death of Amadou Diallo acted criminally, they should be held accountable to the fullest extent of the law.

But if they genuinely believed their lives to be endangered, and the shooting was the result of a tragic misunderstanding, then they should not be railroaded. 026 . 0000.00 00000

The father of the Houston octuplets pleaded no contest yesterday to assaulting his mother-in-law and avoided jail in a deal that will remove the charge from his record if he stays out of trouble. An elderly Nebraska man who fatally shot his 74- year-old wife to save her from what he thought was terminal cancer was told in court yesterday that autopsy results found no trace of cancer. Vernal (Bob) Ohlrich, 76, who pleaded no contest yesterday to manslaughter, said doctors told him the cancer had spread from his wife's colon to lymph nodes. ''I was told by doctors that she should call her children home because she's not going to make it overnight,'' he said.

Amnesty International and the NAACP are demanding explanations, and federal prosecutors are reviewing the shooting last week of a black street pedlar by four white police officers from New York City's elite ''Street Crime Unit,'' whose motto is ''We Own the Night.'' Ethiopian officials, meanwhile, admitted jet fighters were being used to support a counter-offensive. 32 of 73 DOCUMENTS


USA TODAY


February 9, 1999, Tuesday, FINAL EDITION


Overkill


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. 16A


LENGTH: 366 words


In New York City last week, four police officers fired 41 shots
at Amadou Diallo in an apartment vestibule, hitting him 19 times
and killing him where he stood. In Riverside, Calif., last December,
four police officers fired 24 shots at Tyisha Miller in a locked
car, hitting her 12 times and killing her where she sat.

Is there a pattern in these and similar cases that warrants close
examination? Almost certainly.
 

-- In both cases, the victims were black while the officers were
white.

-- In both cases, the shots were excessive.

-- In both cases, the reservoir of good will between law enforcement
and inner-city residents has been drained lower.

Presumably, the officers felt threatened. New York police were
interviewing Diallo in the middle of the night in an especially
dangerous section of the Bronx. In Riverside, reports suggest
that Miller had a firearm on her lap and that she reached toward
it when officers tried to break the car window.

But that's not really good enough. Regardless of what the New
York officers thought, Diallo was unarmed when they killed him.
And Miller was apparently passed out in her locked car. She awakened
to the jolting sounds of someone trying to break in on her.

Officers should indeed take the steps necessary to keep themselves
safe. But when the confrontation is four to one, it takes a lot
to explain why the incident rises to the level of gunplay in the
first place.

So far, there's little evidence to support the insinuations by
community activists that the killings have racial motivations.
Yet it almost doesn't matter. The hard truth is that police-community
relations are defined by the worst instances and that the worst
instances are often defined not by the number of shots fired,
but by the decision to fire at all.

Maybe it is inevitable that front-line police officers will react
as a unit to any perceived threat. But it is also inevitable that
as long as their training invites exaggerated fusillades against
even marginal threats, the infectious rupture of public trust
that characterizes so many inner-city and minority neighborhoods
will continue to fester and weep.

NATION IN BRIEF;
Stardust blasts off with robot on comet quest


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. 10A


LENGTH: 641 words


 

A comet-chasing spacecraft named Stardust rocketed away from Cape Canaveral, Fla., on Sunday on a seven-year, 3 billion-mile quest to bring back precious comet dust. Stardust began its formidable journey one day late, soaring into a clear afternoon sky aboard a Boeing Delta rocket. A last-minute radar problem Saturday caused the delay. NASA's Stardust mission is the first attempt to gather material from beyond the moon and return it to Earth. It is also the first time NASA has sent a robot on an extraterrestrial pickup-and-delivery mission. The last time the agency went after samples in outer space was with the 1972 Apollo 17 mission, the last of the manned lunar landings. Alleged killer gives up after inmate freed A Milwaukee man surrendered Sunday to Chicago police on charges he killed two people in a slaying for which another man was sentenced to death. The state attorney's office, having reviewed a videotaped of an alleged confession by Alstory Simon, issued warrants for both him and his estranged wife, Inez Jackson. Anthony Porter, originally imprisoned for the slayings, spent his first weekend of freedom in 17 years. Porter had been scheduled to die in September, but his appeal was granted two days before the lethal injection.
Microsoft shake-up may be in the works Microsoft Corp. plans to reorganize the No. 1 software maker into four groups and bring back a top programming wizard, who led the development of Windows 95 and the Internet Explorer browser, according to a report published today and an analyst familiar with the plans. News of the reorganization, one of the most sweeping shake-ups in Microsoft's history, could come as early as this week, the Seattle Times reported. The Times said Brad Silverberg, 44, a former Microsoft executive now on leave, will be invited back to head the newly formed consumer group. Silverberg and Microsoft officials weren't immediately available to comment.
Rally decries killing of immigrant by police About 1,000 people rallied in New York on Sunday outside the home of Amadou Diallo, a West African immigrant who died when police fired 41 shots at him. The U.S. Attorney's Office has begun investigating the case, and a grand jury convened by the Bronx district attorney is expected to begin reviewing evidence today. Diallo, 22, who immigrated from Guinea two years ago and worked as a street vendor, died in the vestibule of the building where he lived after being hit 19 times by bullets from four officers early Thursday.
Diet alone may cut blood pressure A study indicates that high blood pressure can be lowered significantly with a healthy diet and without the need for hypertension medication, particularly among blacks. "We are talking about a diet with the potential of preventing and treating hypertension and doing it particularly well in people at risk," said Dr. Laura Svetkey, associate professor of medicine at Duke University Medical Center in Durham, N.C., and lead author of the study. The study is published in today's edition of the Archives of Internal Medicine.
Avalanche kills 1 of 4 trapped near Lake Tahoe An avalanche in California caught four friends and buried them for hours, killing one. The entire Lake Tahoe area has been socked by a series of powerful storms that have dumped almost 2 feet of snow and created blizzard conditions with winds gusting to 100 mph. Many ski resorts in the Sierra Nevada had to close down because of the storms. The avalanche occurred Saturday, trapping the four for six hours.
5 teens found OK after Idaho blizzard Five teenage boys who spent the night lost on a northern Idaho ski resort that was raked by a snowstorm were safe Sunday after three found their way home and two brothers were spotted by snowmobilers. Rescuers said the brothers, ages 13 and 15, appeared to be all right.

The New York Post


February 8, 1999, Monday


GET THE TRUTH, NOT SCAPEGOATS


BYLINE: Bob McManus


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 027


LENGTH: 913 words


'How many trips down darkened alleys does a young cop need to take, ... how many grotesquely bloodied innocent victims of random crime does he need to pack into ambulances, before he begins to wonder far too much about what fate has in store for him?'

PITY the constable, from whom an imperfect city demands perfection. And pity Rudy Giuliani; he needs a friend right now, but he may have used them all up.

First, the cops. They need friends, too.

The death last week of Amadou Diallo, shot 19 times by police in a dingy Bronx rooming house, shocks the conscience.

Somewhat less fearsome - but no less worrisome - was the narrow escape last month of a rap singer who came under police fire in Brooklyn for no apparent good reason.

Is the NYPD out of control?

More to the point, does either event rise to the level of a crime?

These are not unreasonable questions; so far, the answer seems to be no.

True, Diallo appears to have been a straight arrow. A legal immigrant, hard-working and with no criminal record or suspect associates, he regularly sent money to relatives in Guinea and was apparently saving to attend college here.

What Diallo did to attract the attention of four plainclothes street-crime cops remains a mystery. What it was that triggered the 41-shot fusillade that killed him is even less clear.

Somewhat more ambiguous is the case of the Brooklyn rapper. Police say that he was in an erratically driven car in Brooklyn on Jan. 15 - and that when they tried to pull him over, he fired two shots at them. They fired back; happily for the rapper, they missed.

The problem with the police account of events - which was rejected by a grand jury - is that there is no evidence to support it: No gun, no spent shell casings (save their own), no nothing.

Except a bit of irony.

Rap, as a genre, is meant to do explicit violence to civility. And it is no accident that among the more successful rap acts are those which blatantly bait cops.

Mostly, this is harmless stuff. Adolescent suburbanites, usually white, love socially corrosive rap - because it horrifies their parents so - and they're the principal market for it.

Still, they're not likely to shoot cops.

Such music is more problematic in the inner city, where the social contract is a fragile document to begin with - and where white, suburban-raised cops harboring profound suspicions of the people they police can be found in all-too-arrogant abundance.

This makes for a bitter broth, and it has been simmering far too long.

As a practical matter, though, the cops' suspicions aren't unreasonable.

The NYPD doesn't often come into contact with upright citizens - elite street-crime cops least of all.

They know how bitterly they're resented - which is ironic as well, because as bad as conditions can be in high-crime neighorhoods, they would be intolerable without a strong police presence. (When muggers prowl, folks don't call Calvin Butts, now do they?)

Too much can be made - and too often is - of the risks attending police work. But there are too many New York City cops in the ground to lightly dismiss the dangers of the job.

How many trips down darkened alleys does a young cop need to take; how many muttered threats does he need to hear; how many hi-tech handguns does he need to strip from criminals; how many grotesquely wounded innocent victims of random acts of violence does he need to pack into ambulances before he begins to wonder - far too much - about what fate has in store for him?

Some cops can't take it.

For some cops, there comes a night in a cramped vestibule in The Bronx when the safety slips too easily off his own handgun - and shots are fired.

Murder? A crime of any kind?

That's a determination to be made by Bronx DA Bob Johnson - a fellow whose antipathy to the death penalty shouldn't obscure the fact that his integrity and competence deserve far more respect than is being accorded by the U.S. Justice Department, in its eagerness to insinuate itself into the case.

The authorities won't be lacking for advice in the days and weeks just ahead, but they need to maintain perspective. Some cases seem clear-cut - the Abner Louima matter springs to mind - but most aren't.

The DA, and the feds, must set the bar very high. Should they succeed in criminalizing horrific judgment - or, perhaps, simple panic - they will do more than just compound injustice.

They will certify the base assertion that city cops - by implication, all city cops - are natural born killers.

Who prospers with this notion in circulation? Not the cops; but they won't be hurt either - they'll simply go into on-duty hibernation.

No, the criminals will win - and drug dealers in particular.

As always, law-abiding New Yorkers will be the real losers.

Giuliani stands to lose, too - particularly if he doesn't move quickly to correct the obvious training and command breakdowns that underlie these shootings. The legitimacy of his splendid mayoralty is at grave risk.

He has few allies these days, and so he may be reluctant to do what has to be done to establish accountability.

But, just as there is no room for rump justice here, neither is a cover-up of any sort acceptable.

One more thing: New York City is safer today than it has been in more than three decades - and, in the end, the credit for this goes to Giuliani's uncompromising law-and-order sensibilities, and to the NYPD.

This counts for a lot.

The New York Post


February 8, 1999, Monday


1,000 DEMAND OFFICERS BE CHARGED IN SHOOTING


BYLINE: Reported by Ikimulisa Sockwell, Adam Miller and Murray Weiss. Written by Kirsten Danis.


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 002


LENGTH: 361 words


Angry protesters led by the Rev. Al Sharpton crowded into a Bronx neighborhood yesterday to decry the fatal police shooting of a West African immigrant and demand the cops face criminal charges.

About 1,000 people - chanting, carrying signs and demanding justice - jammed the block in front of 1157 Wheeler Ave., where four officers killed an unarmed Amadou Diallo in a hail of 41 bullets Thursday.

"There's no justification for the slaughter of this young man. We want the world to know that we are going to fight for justice," Sharpton told the crowd after leading them in an opening chant of "No justice, no peace."

"We call for the immediate arrest of these four policemen," Sharpton said to cheers and applause.

Two 6-foot-high photos of Diallo - emblazoned with the words, "An innocent man of integrity massacred by police brutality" - hung on buildings behind the speakers, who included Assemblyman Ruben Diaz (D-Bronx) and Iris Baez, mother of cop choke-hold victim Anthony Baez.

"This was murder," Baez said. "We have to stay together. We have to say, 'No more.'"

A cold, wet snow began to fall at the start of the two-hour afternoon rally, but the crowd - which was diverse but made up primarily of Africans and African-Americans from The Bronx - held fast.

Many carried homemade signs saying, "Stop Killer Police," "Cops Have Blood on their Hands" and "KKK Rules NYPD."

Friction with police erupted at one end of the rally when cops arrived to arrest a neighborhood man on an unrelated dispute. But Sharpton urged the crowd to keep calm.

Protesters booed loudly every time speakers mentioned Mayor Giuliani or Police Commissioner Howard Safir, and people in the crowd spontaneously called out for the death penalty for the officers who shot Diallo.

"It's a damn dirty shame!" one man shouted repeatedly.

Another protest is planned for tomorrow in front of the federal courthouse in downtown Manhattan, and a funeral service for Diallo, 22, will be held Friday at the 96th Street mosque, Sharpton said.

RUDY REACHES OUT TO VICTIM'S DAD


BYLINE: Tom Topousis, Murray Weiss and Neil Graves


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 002


LENGTH: 226 words


Mayor Giuliani spoke with the father of a Guinean immigrant killed by cops as the Bronx district attorney got ready to convene a grand jury today to investigate the shooting.

Spokeswoman Colleen Roche would not reveal what the mayor said in his phone conversation with the father of Amadou Diallo, 22, a quiet street merchant shot to death by four cops in front of his apartment Thursday.

Giuliani and police brass also met with Guinea's ambassador to the United States, Mohamed Aly Thiam, and the African nation's ambassador to the United Nations, Roche said.

Bronx District Attorney Robert Johnson will begin presenting evidence today to a grand jury probing last week's shooting.

Diallo, a peddler who worked on 14th Street in Manhattan, came to the United States an illegal immigrant but won his request for political asylum last summer.

The jury is expected to see the medical examiner's report and police reports and hear from Diallo's neighbors and relatives.

But it's still unclear if the four officers who killed Diallo in a barrage of 41 bullets will testify.

Their lawyer, Stephen Worth, has said they want to wait until U.S. Attorney Mary Jo White clarifies what she meant when she said her office would "work closely" with Johnson's.

Worth declined to comment yesterday.

MFUME URGES RENO TO SPEAK ON SHOOTING


BYLINE: Mercury News Wire Services


SECTION: FRONT; Pg. 4A


LENGTH: 175 words


The outcry over the New York killing of an unarmed West African immigrant in a fusillade of 41 police bullets spread to the national stage on Sunday, as the president of the NAACP condemned the shooting as "excessive force at its worst" and urged U.S. Attorney General Janet Reno to reassure Americans that such incidents will not be tolerated.

Kweisi Mfume said he would ask Reno today to make a public statement that "the Justice Department will use every possible vehicle to get to the facts involved in the case and make sure that justice is fair and swift."

Mfume's comments were the first indication that national attention is beginning to focus on the death Thursday of Amadou Diallo. Diallo, a 22-year-old street peddler with no criminal record, was hit by 19 of the 41 bullets fired by four police officers in the vestibule of his Bronx apartment building.

Almost 1,000 people staged an angry rally Sunday in front of the apartment building, chanting "no justice, no peace" and saying the time may have come to "fight back."

USA TODAY


February 8, 1999, Monday, FINAL EDITION


Witnesses to gondola deaths set to face pilot


BYLINE: John Bacon


SECTION: NEWS; Pg. 3A


LENGTH: 565 words


DATELINE: With staff and wire reports


Opening statements are today in the court-martial of Marine Capt.
Richard Ashby, charged in the deaths of 20 people when his jet
snapped a gondola car cable over the Italian village of Cavalese
a year ago.

Twenty-two Italians who say they saw Ashby's jet flying too low
and too fast over the village are expected to be the first witnesses
before a jury of officers at Camp Lejeune, N.C.

At least 21 relatives of victims are coming at the U.S. government's
expense to watch the trial. There are a few seats in the small
courtroom for relatives; a video feed with translators has been
set up in another building.
 

Ashby, 31, of Mission Viejo, Calif., was at the controls of the
EA-6B Prowler jet on Feb. 3, 1998. The North Carolina-based crew
had been assigned to the Aviano, Italy, air base for flights over
Bosnia.

A military investigation blamed the tragedy on reckless flying.
But defense lawyers say Ashby didn't know the cable was strung
across the valley until seconds before hitting it. Ashby faces
a maximum possible sentence of more than 200 years in prison if
convicted on all charges.

HOLOCAUST TALKS: The World Jewish Congress was weighing
possible sanctions against Deutsche Bank and preparing for today's
meeting in Washington with the chairman of Germany's biggest bank
to try to resolve Holocaust claims. Last year, Deutsche Bank announced
a $ 10.1 billion takeover of Bankers Trust as part of an ambitious
expansion drive, but the deal was hamstrung by charges Deutsche
Bank's affiliates used slave labor during World War II. Deutsche
Bank denies using slave laborers, though last week its historian
said the bank helped pay for construction of the Auschwitz death
camp. The WJC wants the bank to make "material reparations"
to Holocaust survivors and heirs.

SHOOTING PROTEST: About 1,000 people rallied outside the
New York home of a West African immigrant who died when police
fired 41 shots at him. Four officers looking for a rapist shot
Amadou Diallo, 22, a street peddler from Guinea, Thursday. Officials
say the officers thought Diallo had a gun, but no gun was found.
The U.S. Attorney's office has begun a probe, and a grand jury
convened by the Bronx prosecutor is expected to begin reviewing
evidence today.

NASA launches pursuit of comet

The comet-chasing spacecraft Stardust rocketed away from Cape
Canaveral on a seven-year, 3 billion-mile quest for precious comet
dust.

NASA's Stardust mission is the first attempt to gather material
from beyond the moon and return it to Earth. It also is the first
time NASA has sent a robot on an extraterrestrial pickup and delivery
mission. The last time the agency went after samples in outer
space was Apollo 17 in 1972, the last of the manned lunar landings.

The launch, aboard a Boeing Delta rocket, came one day after a
last-minute radar problem forced a delay Saturday. Stardust is
to meet up with Comet Wild-2 in January 2004, passing within about
90 miles of the 2 1/2-mile-diameter iceball.

Scientists hope that tiny comet fragments, no bigger than a grain
of salt, will be trapped by a pop-up collector that's covered
with transparent ultralight glass foam called aerogel. The most
vulnerable parts of the 850-pound spacecraft, about the size of
a large refrigerator, are shielded to protect against the swirl
of particles.

The New York Post


February 7, 1999, Sunday


WHY WERE SO MANY BULLETS FIRED?


BYLINE: DOUGALS MONTERO


SECTION: Sport+Late City Final; Pg. 005


LENGTH: 502 words


IT IS easy to see why some people are calling the shooting death of Amadou Diallo by cops a "massacre."

Surveying the vestibule of the two-story Bronx apartment building where Diallo, 24, was shot by cops, the public can observe the following:

*A side wall scarred by about 10 bullets.

*Two wooden doors leading into the red brick building marked by about eight bullet holes or dents.

*The lower part of the inside door panel shattered to bits.

*Bullet scars on the hallway ceiling, walls, the first step of the stairs leading to the second floor and the wall just above the door of Diallo's first-floor apartment.

Simply put, the hard-working, criminally clean immigrant from West Africa never had a chance.

The barrage of gunfire appears to have been so concentrated a large cockroach probably would've been caught in the cross-fire.

What Diallo's friends, neighbors and probably the general law-abiding public wants to know is: Why did four Street Crime Unit cops need 41 bullets to take down an unarmed man?

"I condemn this massacre - I'm sorry, but that's the way it looks," said George Correia, 45, of the United Force, a political party from Diallo's homeland of Guinea.

The four cops - Edward McMellon, Sean Carroll, Kenneth Boss and Richard Murphy - confronted Diallo at 12:43 a.m. just as he was about to walk out of his building at 1157 Wheeler St.

Standing outside, the cops began shooting through the 3-foot-wide building entrance after Diallo acted in a suspicious manner, their union lawyer has said.

Whether the cops were justified for using deadly force is debatable and best left up to investigators.

In fairness to the cops - who haven't spoken to investigators - I'm told a quick trigger finger could empty a 9mm. semiautomatic handgun, which carries 16 bullets, in four seconds.

What may disturb some folks, however, is that 19 bullets struck Diallo.

That means 22 bullets went crashing helter-skelter throughout the building's first floor, possibly endangering other lives.

"All I know it could've been anybody," said Diallo's visibly shaken next- door neighbor, Kemrajie Bassit, thinking about her 24-year-old son getting caught in the crossfire.

Mamadou A. Diallo, 31, a cousin of the dead man, said he trusts the justice system, but in light of the mistake and number of bullets fired he "definitely" wants the officers tested for drugs and alcohol.

However, the department does not test for either after a deadly shooting, said Detective Walter Burnes, an NYPD spokesman.

Subway motormen involved in fatal incidents are tested for drugs and alcohol. Maybe the Police Department should consider such a policy to allay the concerns of Kathleen Celestine, 47, who also can't understand why so many shots were fired.

"The first thing that came to my mind when I heard about it was they [the cops] must've been high."

And like a chorus, five of the eight people standing outside Diallo's bullet-scarred building said, "I thought so, too."


LOAD-DATE: February 9, 1999


LANGUAGE: ENGLISH


Copyright 1999 N.Y.P. Holdings, Inc. All rights reserved.

Mamadou A. Diallo, 31, a cousin of the dead man, said he trusts the justice system, but in light of the mistake and number of bullets fired he "definitely" wants the officers tested for drugs and alcohol.

However, the department does not test for either after a deadly shooting, said Detective Walter Burnes, an NYPD spokesman.

Subway motormen involved in fatal incidents are tested for drugs and alcohol. Maybe the Police Department should consider such a policy to allay the concerns of Kathleen Celestine, 47, who also can't understand why so many shots were fired.

"The first thing that came to my mind when I heard about it was they [the cops] must've been high."

And like a chorus, five of the eight people standing outside Diallo's bullet-scarred building said, "I thought so, too."

The New York Post


February 7, 1999, Sunday


COPS WILLING TO TALK: LAWYER : DA PREPS FOR GRAND JURY


BYLINE: Reported by Murray Weiss, Angela C. Allen, Rocco Parascandola and Maggie Haberman. Written by Allen Salkin.


SECTION: Sport+Late City Final; Pg. 005


LENGTH: 527 words


The cops who killed an unarmed immigrant in a flurry of 41 bullets are so confident their actions were justified, they're willing to testify before a grand jury, their lawyer said yesterday.

Bronx District Attorney Robert Johnson is scheduled to convene a grand jury in the shooting death of Amadou Diallo, 24, tomorrow.

The cops who killed the Guinean sidewalk merchant Thursday morning - Sean Carroll, 35, Edward McMellon, 26, Kenneth Boss, 27 and Richard Murphy, 26 - have not yet spoken to investigators, but their lawyer Stephen Worth said they are prepared to speak to a grand jury this week.

But he added there is no point for them to testify for the Bronx grand jury if the case is going to be completely taken over by the U.S. attorney's office before a state criminal trial can occur.

U.S. Attorney Mary Jo White has announced that her office will "work closely" with Johnson's office - but she has not been more specific.

The federal government took over the Abner Louima assault case before a state trial, but tried Francis Livoti for the choking death of Anthony Baez after Livoti was acquitted by the state.

Worth suggested Diallo's lack of English may have contributed to his brutal death because he may not have understood what cops meant by "freeze."

But people who knew the gentle, hard-working man said his English was fine.

"This guy speaks English," said an uncle Mamadou Diallo. "He was educated before he came. He learned to speak English before he came here."

Another relative said Diallo had a stutter - which might explain a possible delay in his response to the commands of cops working for a unit whose motto is "We Own the Night."

"It would take him a long time to say one word, but he's an intelligent person," Mamadouba Diallo said.

Attorney General Eliot Spitzer and state Sen. David Paterson (D-Harlem) said the department needs to reconsider its now- standard use of the quick-firing 9 mm semi-automatic gun - used in the Diallo slaying.

As community outrage continued to swell over the shooting, the Police Department released statistics showing cops are using their guns less frequently.

The NYPD report showed that less than 1 percent of the city's 39,000-strong force discharged their weapons last year, killing 19 people.

In 1998, there were 249 firearm-discharge incidents compared with 253 in 1997, 317 in 1996 and 344 in 1995.

Mayor Giuliani said the city would help pay to bring Diallo's relatives to the United States.

Giuliani used the statistics to defend the Police Department in the wake of the Diallo horror.

"This individual incident is one of great concern, and it's one that should be investigated very thoroughly," he said.

"But it would be very unfair to jump from this incident to an entire Police Department."

Carroll, McMellon, Boss and Murphy had been patrolling the area around Diallo's home in hopes of gathering information about both a serial rapist in the area and a robbery-murder of a livery driver last week.


LOAD-DATE: February 9, 1999


LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

St. Louis Post-Dispatch (Missouri)


February 7, 1999, Sunday, EARLY FIVE STAR EDITION


NATION  


BYLINE: From News Services


SECTION: NEWS, Pg. A5, NATION BRIEFS COLUMN


LENGTH: 1087 words


 
 
Chicken burritos supplied to airline are recalled

Thousands of chicken burritos made by a division of Tyson Foods Inc., have been recalled because of listeria, a potentially deadly bacteria. The recall affects some 78,000 burritos supplied to American Airlines at the end of last year, according to the Detroit Free Press. The subsidiary supplies food for airlines and makes foods for restaurants. It ships about 50 million meals a year, the paper said Saturday. Listeria was found in burritos sampled in Detroit, the paper said. When ingested, the bacteria c an cause a disease called listeriosis that starts out resembling intestinal flu but can lead to death. Listeriosis primarily affects fetuses, the very young, the elderly and people with weakened immune systems. Healthy people are not normally sickened by it. Other recent food product recalls include hot dogs and deli meats made by Bil Mar Foods, a unit of Sara Lee Corp. A nationwide outbreak of listeria was linked to Bil-Mar's Zeeland, Mich., plant.
 
GOP vows to save Social Security and not cut benefits

Rep. Paul Ryan, R-Wis., said Saturday that Republicans were committed to saving the Social Security system without cutting retirement benefits. In the weekly Republican radio address, Ryan announced that he would introduce legislation next week seeking to guarantee existing benefit levels to all Social Security recipients. "We in Congress are absolutely committed that, no matter what other reforms need to be made to make the program solvent in the future, there will be no change in benefits to current or soon-to-be retirees," Ryan said. "The House leadership has pledged to move swiftly on this important measure in the next few weeks because we want Americans of all ages to know exactly where we stand." President Bill Clinton already is seeking to set aside 62 percent of the federal budget surplus - expected to total $ 2.41 trillion over the next decade - to shore up the Social Security system. tt1 Republicans support using most of the surpluses for Social Security. But House and Senate Republican leaders insist that budget surpluses would provide enough cash for Social Security and for income tax cuts of at least 10 percent.
 
Police shooting of immigrant will be investigated

The Justice Department and New York prosecutors say they will investigate the death of an immigrant from Guinea. The victim was killed in a police shooting. Four officers fired 41 shots at Amadou Diallo, 22, who was struck 19 times in the vestibule of his Bronx home, according to the medical examiner's report. U.S. Attorney Mary Jo White said Friday that her office would investigate the case along with the Bronx district attorney's office. Relatives and friends encouraged federal officials to review the shooting, which they called an execution. "This was not a police murder; it was a police slaughter," said the Rev. Al Sharpton. "If a man was put in front of a firing squad, he would not expect to be shot at 41 times." Some have suggested race was a possible factor for the shooting on Thursday. Diallo was black and the four officers are white. ma Police Commissioner Howard Safir said officials still don't know exactly what happened because there were no civilian witnesses and investigators have not talked to the officers yet. The Bronx's district attorney has asked police not to interview the officers until their investigation is completed. "They took what they felt to be, and feel to be, appropriate police action," said the officers' lawyer, Stephen Worth. lots
 
Five are killed when private plane crashes after takeoff

A private plane crashed shortly after takeoff from the Ozona, Texas, airport Saturday, killing all five people aboard, officials reported. Wreckage of the plane, identified as a Cessna 182, was found Saturday morning on a pasture near the airport in southwest Texas. The victims were three adults and two children, said the Department of Public Safety's spokesman, Mike Cox, in Austin. All were dead at the scene. He said the victims were related, but he gave no identities. The plane had been reported missing after it took off about 9 p.m. Friday, according to Cox.
 
Geologist finds earthquake fault near San Francisco

A geologist has discovered an earthquake fault in the seaside cliffs just south of San Francisco. The fault, though unknown until recently, has been around for a long time. It's not clear if it is active, geologist Scott Kieffer said last week. Kieffer was mapping the Mussel Rock area of Daly City last fall when he roped down the side of a cliff to examine layers of sedimentary rock and found clear evidence of a quake across a previously unmapped fault. He dated the sedimentary layer around the rupture to 120,000 years ago, which means the state Division of Mines and Geology would classify it as inactive. But the fault would be considered active if it has generated a quake as recently as 11,000 years ago, and would require urgent work to survey its seismic danger.
 
Overturning of rapist's sentence upsets prosecutor

A prosecutor in Cincinnati said Saturday that he was appalled by an appeals court decision to overturn a prison sentence for a child rapist because the judge had quoted from the Bible when imposing the punishment. In a 2-1 verdict, the Ohio 1st District Court of Appeals reversed on Friday a 51-year sentence for James Arnett, 33. He had pleaded guilty a year ago to 10 counts of rape involving the 8-year-old daughter of his fiancee. Hamilton County Prosecutor Mike Allen said, "For 200 years in this country, judges have been invoking Scripture in the courtroom. I think this reversal of sentence is total nonsense." In its majority opinion, the appeals court said Judge Melba Marsh clearly was influenced in her sentencing by a passage from the Bible. She, therefore, denied the defendant his right to due process by violating the guarantee of separation of church and state, the ruling said. "By factoring in religion, the court is acting outside of Ohio's sentencing guidelines, as well as violating the offender's due process rights," Judge Mark Painter wrote in his majority opinion. Marsh said in the courtroom that she had used the Bible as "an additional source of help" in determining the severity of the sentence. Allen said he would try to make sure Arnett stayed in prison until the case goes back to Marsh's court for a new sentence. He likely will appeal the decision to the Ohio Supreme Court, he said.

Spacecraft launching is canceled 2 minutes from liftoff

Launch managers had the narrowest of windows - just one second - in which to ignite its rocket and boost the phone-booth size, 848-pound robotic spacecraft on its way to a meeting with Comet Wild-2. The alarm indicated a momentary dip in current to a radar beacon used to track the spacecraft. NASA officials were trying to decide whether to attempt to launch at 3:04 p.m. Sunday St. Louis time.

The New York Post


February 6, 1999, Saturday


WE NEED ANSWERS - FAST ; FIND OUT WHAT HAPPENED SO IT DOESN'T HAPPEN AGAIN


BYLINE: MURRAY WEISS


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 005


LENGTH: 698 words


THE killing of Amadou Diallo has hit a raw nerve with New Yorkers.

Four cops against one man. Forty-one shots fired. He's unarmed. Not a suspect in a crime. No criminal record. Outside his Bronx apartment.

Regardless of where you sit on the police spectrum, the shooting by Street Crime Unit cops is as disturbing as allegations cops sodomized Abner Louima with a stick in a precinct bathroom.

Honest friends of the NYPD woke up yesterday concerned about how cops could kill a man who had just come home from a day's work.

Even the best of New York's Finest have been jarred by the tragedy.

"It's f---in' horrible," one of the brightest police officials said of a worst-case scenario that provides a radioactive bomb for critics, wherever they stand.

"It looks as bad as you can make it look," another official, a former Street Crime Unit supervisor, sadly acknowledged. "It polarizes people."

He believes the incident "will become one of those critical points in New York policing." And in a lot of ways, it should.

But the rabble-rousers, who are calling the shooting an outright "execution," are ignoring reality.

Reasonable officials remain open-minded about the real truths that occur during heart-pumping moments when cops, sent out to confront the city's worst criminals, are forced to make split-second decisions.

If you've seen the movie "Saving Private Ryan," you can understand the tension.

Members of the NYPD's vaunted Street Crime Unit show up for work willing to risk their lives in neighborhoods with the highest crime.

Wherever they are deployed, crime drops precipitously.

The unit's track record speaks for itself - great restraint, huge arrest numbers, disproportionate seizures of weapons.

They are heavily supervised. Rarely fire weapons. And several of their Finest, such as Kevin Gillespie, have made the ultimate sacrifice.

But they remain human - and the very high-risk nature of their work is ripe for tragedy.

It's just not supposed to be tragic for some guy standing innocently outside his home after a long day's work.

"I have more questions here than answers," the ex-SCU boss said. "But questions are easy. It's the answers that are difficult and the solutions even harder."

Could race have been a factor? The cops are white, Diallo black.

"I don't think so. We take guns from bad guys, whether they are white, black, green or purple," the ex-SCU boss said.

Were the cops - from a unit that proudly proclaims "We Own the Night" - overly aggressive?

"I have never seen that," the boss said.

Does the tragedy expose a flaw in police tactics?

"It is hard for me to understand what was in their minds until there are answers," he said.

A couple of years ago, several anti-crime cops in Brooklyn shot and killed a man in a stolen car who they thought was reaching for a gun.

There was no gun, but the man was wanted for murder.

The Rev. Al Sharpton led marches. But the grand jury did not indict.

New Yorkers also must understand that this is not another Abner Louima.

The shooting in The Bronx occurred, tragically, in the course of real police work.

"No one set out to hurt that man that night," the top cop said. "I do not like what happened Thursday morning, but it is not allegedly brutalizing someone in a bathroom, which was criminality.

"This is a tactical problem," he added.

Mayor Giuliani struck the right tone yesterday, saying: "I understand the feelings of frustration and fear and anger.

"I also think it shameful that people exploit or implicate the entire Police Department," Giuliani said, appealing for people to await Bronx DA Robert Johnson's investigation.

The bottom line remains that the NYPD has to find out how this happened - and find a way to prevent it from happening again.

The New York Post


February 6, 1999, Saturday


OFFICERS SAY THEY 'FEEL TERRIBLE' ; FOUR COPS OFFER KIN CONDOLENCES AFTER KILLING MAN IN HAIL OF BULLETS


BYLINE: Reported by Kieran Crowley, Charles Sussman, Jesse Angelo, David Luff, Murray Weiss and Allen Salkin. Written by Salkin.


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 002


LENGTH: 707 words


"On a human level, these officers are sorry that Amadou Diallo died."LAWYER STEPHEN WORTH

The cops who blew away unarmed immigrant Amadou Diallo in a rain of 41 bullets want his family to know they "feel terrible" about the tragedy.

"They want to send their personal condolences to the family and friends of Mr. Diallo," James Savage, acting head of the Patrolmen's Benevolent Association, said yesterday.

"The cops feel terrible," Savage said.

The officers - Sean Carroll, 35; Edward McMellon, 26; Kenneth Boss, 27; and Richard Murphy, 26 - gathered for a meeting with their lawyer in Long Island yesterday before going their separate ways.

Those seen in public were dealing with their shredded emotions in different ways.

Officer Carroll used his fists to grind the tears from his eyes as he left his Long Island home - a day after emptying his gun into the 24-year-old Diallo.

"I'm sorry, but under the circumstances, I can't talk now," Carroll, 35, sobbed.

"Please leave my family alone."

Savage's account of the four cops' feelings was more direct than the version offered by their lawyer, Stephen Worth.

"On a human level, these officers are sorry that Amadou Diallo died," Worth said.

"However, they feel that they took appropriate police action under the circumstances. It may well develop that Amadou Diallo had a language barrier which prevented him from following the officers' orders."

Officer Murphy said nothing as he hurried away from his Fresh Meadows, Queens home early in the day with his wife and 3-month-old boy in tow.

The Murphys put their new baby into his car seat in an old sedan parked on the street, then climbed in and sped away.

A neighbor who asked not to be named said Murphy moved into the neighborhood 18 months ago.

"He was so excited about the birth of his baby three months ago," said the neighbor.

"It's a beautiful baby boy and he was very proud of it."

The Murphys' two-story, brick row house on a grassy circle in the planned community sat silent the rest of the day.

Neighbor Joe Rivetti said Murphy is normally a calm and friendly person, respected in the area.

"He always stops to say hello to the old people on the block and make sure they're all right," Rivetti said.

"My son wants to be a cop and Richard was very helpful giving him pointers about joining the auxiliary police and the cadet force," Rivetti said.

No one answered the door at the yellow Kings Park, L.I., home of Officer Boss - but someone called Suffolk County cops to chase away reporters.

The green-shuttered Cape Cod-style home surrounded by a white picket fence gazes out on the waters of Long Island Sound.

Neighbor Arnold Discorsi, 63, tried to make sense of the tragedy.

"It's unbelievable that he would shoot an unarmed man," Discorsi said, speculating that Diallo, from Guinea in West Africa, might have misunderstood cops' commands or made a sudden move that brought on the gunfire.

"I believe they were shooting for a good reason, even if it was an accident."

In Babylon, L.I., a neighbor said Carroll has lived in the same, two-story, gold-colored house his whole life.

"We have a block party every year and he organizes it," Linda Gugliuzzo said. "I can't tell you anything but nice things about the guy."

Carroll is married with one son and one daughter who attends art classes with Gugliuzzo's granddaughter.

A large white and blue boat was up on concrete blocks in the driveway of the Carroll home.

Sean Carroll could not hold back the tears as he apologized to a reporter for not being able to say any more.

"I have an appointment," he choked.

"They're very nice people," Lee Levitt said. "They're very fine people. Basically they keep to themselves."

McMellon worked in the 75th Precinct prior to his assignment to the Street Crime Unit.

A supervisor at the 75th described McMellon as an active cop who made numerous gun arrests in East New York.

Pending the investigation of the shooting, the four officers have been removed from street patrols and given administrative assignments.

GRAPHIC: TOO MUCH: Officer Sean Carroll sobs uncontrollably outside his Babylon, L.I., home yesterday when asked about the shooting.Veronique Louis

The New York Post


February 6, 1999, Saturday


FEDS TO JOIN PROBE AS FAMILY BEGS FOR JUSTICE ; FEDS TO JOIN CIVIL-RIGHTS PROBE


BYLINE: Reported by Frankie Edozien, Rocco Parascandola, Angela C. Allen, Murray Weiss, David Seifman, Larry Celona and Allen Salkin. Written by Salkin.


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 002


LENGTH: 589 words


Grief-stricken relatives of the unarmed West African immigrant killed in a hail of police bullets called for justice yesterday as the feds said they'll join the probe of the tragic shooting.

Amadou Diallo, 24, was hit by 19 of the 41 bullets fired by four plainclothes police officers early Thursday in the well-lit vestibule of his Bronx apartment building, the city medical examiner said.

Internal affairs investigators working with the Bronx district attorney's office combed the area around 1157 Wheeler St. in what sources said has been a fruitless search for a civilian witness to the shooting.

They have found three witnesses to events either before or after the shooting, sources said.

U.S. Attorney Mary Jo White said her office will work closely with the office of Bronx D.A. Robert Johnson to look for possible civil-rights violations.

The four officers who fired the shots are white; Diallo is black.

A prayer vigil and rally was slated for 2 p.m. tomorrow outside the victim's home and supporters plan to march Tuesday in front of White's office to urge federal involvement in the case.

"We just want these people to face the music and pay the price for what they did," said Diallo's cousin, Modousalieu Jallovv, who flew in from Detroit.

"He worked hard to take care of his family."

Diallo's father, a businessman working in Vietnam, plans to accompany his son's body home Feb. 13 to Lelouma, a small village in Guinea.

The officers who shot Diallo, Sean Carroll, 35, Edward McMellon, 26, Kenneth Boss, 27, and Richard Murphy 26, had not yet been questioned by investigators yesterday, but they met with lawyer Stephen Worth.

A source familiar with their accounts of the shooting said they were patrolling the Soundview section of The Bronx because a livery cab driver had been robbed and murdered there earlier in the week. Earlier reports indicated they were looking for a murderous serial rapist.

They spotted Diallo walking in and out of his building, got out of their car and ordered him to freeze, the source said.

Diallo moved his hands toward his belt area, possibly removing something from his pocket and beginning to lift it, the source said.

Then came the bullets - 41 of them in just a few seconds.

The whole tragedy - from the time cops got out of their car until the last bullet flew - lasted less than 2 minutes.

Diallo was found dead, a wallet and a beeper beside him.

Mayor Giuliani called the Guinean consul to the United Nations, promising to keep him informed, and dispatched a community-assistance unit to help the dead man's relatives.

"We're very concerned about the number of shots, but we have no facts yet from which anyone can draw a conclusion," Giuliani said.

Investigators interviewed 13 cops today who worked the neighborhood after the shooting - seven from the Street Crime Unit and six from the 43rd precinct.

The Philadelphia Inquirer


FEBRUARY 6, 1999 Saturday D EDITION


BRONX POLICE KILLING LEAVES QUESTIONS / FOUR OFFICERS FIRED 41 BULLETS AT AN UNARMED MAN. OFFICIALS AND ACTIVISTS ARE SEEKING ANSWERS.


BYLINE: Henry Goldman, INQUIRER STAFF WRITER


SECTION: NATIONAL; Pg. A02


LENGTH: 958 words


DATELINE: NEW YORK


Officials and activists sought answers yesterday in the killing Thursday of an unarmed West African immigrant by police, who fired 41 bullets at him as he stood in the narrow vestibule of his Bronx apartment building.

The dead man, Amadou "Ahmed" Diallo, 22, had no criminal record and was widely praised by neighbors, family and friends yesterday as a devout Muslim who neither smoked nor drank, and who peddled hats, gloves and scarves on 14th Street in lower Manhattan so he could send money to his family in Guinea.

The officers have not yet been questioned, but Mayor Rudolph Giuliani asked for patience while an investigation by Bronx District Attorney Robert Johnson goes forward. "We all have to wait and react to the facts," the mayor said.

Diallo "was a good man who prayed, worked, and enjoyed the company of his friends," Alpha Balde, 38, a neighbor who knew him from Africa, said. "He wanted to go to school to learn computers, and he never got the chance. They broke his life. We're all shocked about it."

Balde spoke outside the now-bullet-scarred door of 1157 Wheeler Ave., a two-story brick rowhouse on a tidy street reminiscent of some of the economically sturdier blocks of Olney or Logan in Philadelphia.

On the door and inside the vestibule, marked and labeled, was evidence of more than two dozen bullet holes. A small candle flickered in the corner where Diallo's head had rested; a single red rose had been stuck in the cracks between the exposed brick, where bullets had shattered a plaster wall.

Inside the apartment house, bullet holes marred the ceiling and staircase in the hall. There were dried drops of blood on the first-floor carpet.

An autopsy released yesterday found that Diallo had been hit by 19 bullets: 11 in the legs, 5 in the torso, and 1 each in the back, right arm and chest.

"The key issue here is the number of bullets," said Norman Siegel of the New York Civil Liberties Association, who served on a police-brutality task force empaneled in 1997 after the alleged beating and torture by police of Abner Louima, a Haitian immigrant. "What circumstances would justify 41, even if the suspect had a weapon?"

Police rules on the use of lethal force are clear: Officers may fire when they or their partners have reason to fear for their lives or the lives of others. They are to hold fire once a target has been disabled.

Yesterday, Sidique Wai, who heads an organization of African immigrants to the United States, called for a federal investigation of the New York Police Department. As he spoke in front of the shooting site, he was joined by Inez and Ramon Baez, whose son, Anthony, was choked to death by an officer in 1994.

Amnesty International called the killing of Diallo "distressing." And black activist Al Sharpton has begun organizing a rally to be held at the site tomorrow.

The four officers involved in the shooting were part of a plainclothes street-crime squad that has been credited citywide with collecting about 40 percent of the illegal handguns seized by police over the past several years. The unit had been assigned to look for a serial rapist in the Bronx.

Yesterday, critics of the police department, including representatives from a group of African American officers, said the street-crime unit had been allowed to be too aggressive on patrol.

"I'm troubled because there appears to be a comfort level on the part of Mayor Giuliani and the police commissioner with very aggressive tactics, all in the name of getting guns off the street, and it makes incidents like this inevitable," said Lt. Eric Adams, an officer assigned to the 94th Precinct in Brooklyn who is president of One Hundred Blacks in Law Enforcement.

Adams said that he went to the scene of the shooting yesterday to express his outrage. "The entire city should be upset," he said. "When you look inside this cramped, narrow hallway, you wonder how four cops got in here, and how they could shoot off so many rounds. It's a miracle they didn't kill each other, and others asleep in the building."

The officers' attorney, Stephen Worth, said that the shooting was explainable and not racist violence. "They took what they felt to be - and feel to be - appropriate police action," he said. "I think it will likely develop that Mr. Diallo had a language barrier, which prevented him from understanding and complying with the officers' orders, and made him continue to act in an aggressive manner toward the police officers."

Neighbors yesterday recalled how they heard what sounded like machine-gun fire coming from the entry of the modest apartment in the middle of the block early Thursday. "It's something we don't ever hear or see," Jimmy Graham, 48, who has lived there for 24 years, said. "This is a block of hard working, lawful people; we never have problems."

The four officers involved in the shooting - Edward McMellon, 26, Sean Carroll, 35, Kenneth Boss, 27, and Richard Murphy, 26, were equipped with 9-mm semiautomatic pistols, which hold 16 bullets and can be completely emptied within four seconds. They have all been placed on administrative duty while the investigation continues.

McMellon, Carroll and Boss have been involved in other shootings in the past two years. McMellon and Carroll were found to have acted properly, police said. Boss remains under investigation for an Oct. 31, 1997, shooting in Brooklyn. According to the Civilian Complaint Review Board, McMellon was the subject of five complaints, while Carroll and Boss have each received three.

As of yesterday, none of the four officers had been interviewed, because police-union procedures afford officers a 48-hour "cooling off" period during which they can refuse to be questioned.

Unarmed man killed in police volley

An unarmed West African immigrant with no criminal record was killed early Thursday morning by four New York City police officers who fired 41 shots at him in the doorway of his Bronx apartment building, police said. The ruling by the world's leading astronomical organization came amid a brewing cosmic storm among scientists and stargazers, afraid that the smallest planet in the solar system was being demoted. News reports had said Pluto was to be demoted to a Minor Planet, or --- worse --- a Trans-Neptunian Object. That simply isn't so, the Paris-based International Astronomical Union said Thursday.

The New York Post


February 5, 1999, Friday


COPS GUN DOWN UNARMED MAN - 41 BULLETS FIRED AT BX. 'SUSPECT'


BYLINE: Reported by Murray Weiss, Rocco Parascandola, David Seifman, Angela C. Allen, Frankie Edozien and Allyson Lieberman. Written by Cathy Burke.


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 004


LENGTH: 737 words


Four veteran cops from an elite anti-crime unit blasted 41 bullets at an unarmed Bronx man yesterday - insisting later they thought the young African immigrant was reaching for a gun.

The 12:44 a.m. slaying of Amadou Diallo, 24, stunned both the tight-knit Guinean community in Soundview and police brass.

Next to Diallo's bullet-riddled body - which may have been struck as many as 22 times - cops found only a beeper and a wallet with some money inside.

"It's a tragedy from every perspective," one police official said. "Maybe the guy made a move. Maybe he thought he was gonna get robbed. Either way, there's a lot of shooting. It's one of those tragedies."

Friends of the slain man, an immigrant from Guinea who sold gloves and hats on the streets of Manhattan, said they would meet today to begin raising funds to send his body home to Guinea.

"This is so sad, my whole body shakes," said Saidou Diallo, 33, who is not related but comes from the same village in Guinea in West Africa.

"I'm trying not to be angry - I can't even be angry, because it's just so sad."

Mayor Giuliani said it might be two or three days before facts of the case are clear.

"Be patient," he urged Soundview residents.

"We all have to wait and react to facts. We've had terrible mistakes in this city when people have reacted to rumors and intuition and feelings. Let's let the process take its course and react to facts."

According to investigators, four Street Crime Unit officers - Richard Murphy, 26, Sean Carroll, 35, Edward McMellon, 26, and Kenneth Boss, 27 - were searching for the killer Bronx Rapist, a suspect who has committed 36 rapes, robberies and at least one murder since August 1993.

Officials concede there was no call to Diallo's apartment building on Wheeler Avenue around midnight yesterday - and that address wasn't the location of any previous attacks by the alleged rapist.

What happened next is unclear, since no witnesses came forward.

The mayor noted that the officers involved do not have to speak, based on a union provision called the "48-Hour Rule," which gives cops in a possible brutality case two days before they have to talk to investigators.

But the Diallo family lawyer, Kyle Watters, said the young immigrant, who came to the United States 2 years ago, had come home after a 12-hour work day and then left to pick something up at a nearby store.

When he returned, he was confronted by the four cops. But it isn't clear what, if anything, was said.

Sources who know the cops say the four thought Diallo was reaching for a weapon in his waistband - though no gun was found.

"They feel terrible about this," one source said.

Carroll and McMellon emptied their 9mm semiautomatic pistols - 16 rounds apiece.

Murphy fired four times, and Boss shot five times, officials said.

"All you could see was gunsmoke in the air," said across-the-street neighbor Nilsa Ortiz.

"He had nothing that could be construed as a gun," Watters said. "He was doing nothing suspicious."

Roommate Abdo Rahman Diallo, who is not related, said he was awoken by cops who demanded he come and identify Diallo's body.

"I didn't hear anything, but then the police knocked on the door," he said. "They said, 'Come with us.'

"The police said to me, 'Do you know this guy? I said, 'Yes, we live together.' I was scared."

Diallo's body was taken to Jacobi Hospital, and he will be buried in his native country, said his uncle, Mamadou Diallo.

He died of multiple gunshot wounds to the torso, said Ellen Borakove, spokeswoman for the medical examiner's office. The medical examiner was still determining how many bullets hit Diallo and the direction they were fired from. The information was expected by today. Sources said they believe the young man was hit 22 times.
