The New York Post


February 5, 1999, Friday


VICTIM WAS TRYING FOR 'BETTER LIFE'; HE TRIED TO BUILD 'A BETTER LIFE'


BYLINE: ANGELA C. ALLEN and CATHY BURKE


SECTION: All Editions; Pg. 004


LENGTH: 471 words


Amadou Diallo was living the American Dream.

Friends say he died the American Nightmare - wrongly cut down by overzealous cops.

"He came here for a better life," said Diallo's uncle, Mamadou Diallo, 38.

"He was so excited to come here. He hoped to help his family get here one day because the life in America here is so good."

Amadou Diallo, a 24-year-old native of Leloume, a village in Guinea, West Africa, had been living for the past year with two roommates in a cramped apartment in a tight-knit Bronx community of his countrymen.

Although he worked grueling, 12-hour days as a street vendor, peddling gloves and hats on 14th Street in Manhattan, there were signs Diallo was finding his way in the new culture.

And loving it.

A poster of movie star Leonardo DiCaprio adorned one wall of the small three-room apartment on Wheeler Street in the Soundview section that he shared with his roommates. The young men traded tapes of their favorite music.

The uncle recalled how an ambitious Diallo arrived in the United States 2 years ago, with barely more than his dreams and youthful energy.

For a while, Diallo, a devout Muslim who didn't smoke, drink or take drugs, stayed with his uncle in Soundview.

"He was never a problem, never a problem," the uncle said.

Diallo was quiet, some friends said, because a stammer made speaking difficult at times. He would stamp his foot in exasperation - almost as if the gesture would force the words to come, said a friend, Cellou Diallo, 24, who is no relation.

"Even though he didn't talk too much, he'd walk across the street just to say hello," he said.

When Amadou wasn't working, he loved to listen to Alpha Blondy, a reggae singer, play soccer or attend a local mosque.

"He was a good worker and very law-abiding," said his uncle, who last spoke to his nephew when both went to their mosque to pray at the end of the holy month of Ramadan.

Yesterday, Amadou Diallo's friends and relatives struggled with the nightmarish end to the young man's life.

Diallo, who was unarmed, was killed when cops fired 41 bullets at him in the hallway of his apartment building, apparently believing he was reaching for a weapon.

"We need an explanation," said family friend Demba Sanyang, 39.

"It looks like a tragic mistake on the part of the police. I am very angry. The police had no reason to shoot someone [so many times]. It's outrageous."

Kyle Watters, who will represent the Diallo family and who also represents the Guinean Association of America, said the victim's relatives "want justice."

"It's not right that he gets sent back to his country in a wooden box," Watters said. "He was looking for a better life, not a shorter one."

The New York Times


February 11, 1999, Thursday, Late Edition - Final


In America;
A Brewing Storm


BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT


SECTION: Section A; Page 33; Column 5; Editorial Desk


LENGTH: 695 words


The woman stood outside the tiny vestibule on Wheeler Avenue in the Bronx where Amadou Diallo had been killed in an astonishing barrage of gunfire unleashed by four undercover cops. A cold wind was blowing down the quiet street. Candles flickered on the floor of the unlocked vestibule. Flowers had also been placed on the floor. The woman made the sign of the cross and then wiped tears from her face. But she couldn't stop crying.

I was standing a few feet away with a notebook and a pen. The woman turned to me, still wiping her eyes, and said, "For the first time in my life I feel hatred."

The next day, at a rally in Foley Square in lower Manhattan, a brokerage house employee named Gordon Andrews said: "This expletive has got to stop. They are killing us and nobody is hearing us."

A lawyer from Brooklyn who stood on the steps of the State Supreme Court Building to watch the rally told me: "There's a storm brewing, and it's a big one. People are fed up. I'm fed up."

I don't know if Mayor Rudolph Giuliani realizes it, but there are limits to the amount of abuse that black New Yorkers will accept from violent and racist police officers. And those limits are fast approaching.

Black New Yorkers are in a fury over the cold-blooded killing of Mr. Diallo. His death is seen by most blacks as simply the latest tragic manifestation of the ruthless and humiliating treatment of ethnic minorities that is part of the daily routine of so many cops in this city.

Apologists for the Police Department can try to sugar-coat it if they like. Yes, it's true that crime has dropped precipitously. Yes, it's true that most cops are solid citizens. But it is also true that there is a frightening number of violent, racist, sadistic and in some cases homicidal police officers who spend much of their time terrorizing people. And that behavior is widely seen by black New Yorkers as being tolerated if not condoned by Mayor Giuliani.

How gruesome has the situation become? Some parents and civic leaders are teaching black and Hispanic children to quickly display their hands during any encounter with the police, like little criminals. This is to show that the youngsters are not armed and therefore should not be blown into eternity at age 10 or 15 or 20 by a trigger-happy stranger in a blue uniform.

There has been a long epidemic of outrages -- killings, the torturing of Abner Louima, the invasions by rampaging cops of the apartments of innocent families, the routine beating and harassment of young men and boys, the curses and the racial slurs, the arrests on phony charges of individuals who dare to object to abusive treatment, and more.

Last summer the Mayor ordered an entire section of Harlem locked down because he objected to a rally that was being held.

There is a widespread feeling among black New Yorkers that they are living in a police state, and that many of the cops are a threat to the very lives of their children. The anger and resentment over this is growing by the day. And increasingly the resentment is being directed not just toward the police and the Mayor but toward white New Yorkers in general, who are not subjected to the same levels of brutality and harassment.

"I am scared to death," said a former city official, who asked not to be identified, "that this division between blacks and whites, this schism, is growing so large that it can't be repaired. African-Americans perceive that the white community largely condones Rudy's policies as they relate to the blacks and the police. And therefore the whites are as much to blame as Rudy and the police."

I don't believe that most white New Yorkers condone police misconduct, but there have not been nearly enough white voices raised against the atrocities committed by the police. This unfortunate silence is, indeed, a form of encouragement.

The cops who killed Amadou Diallo have had several days now to craft their story. The Mayor and the Police Commissioner are doing their public-relations thing, offering condolences, ordering meaningless training, waiting for the press to turn its attention elsewhere.

Meanwhile, black New Yorkers are seething.

The New York Times


February 11, 1999, Thursday, Late Edition - Final


Mayor Offers New Statistics In Defense Of the Police


BYLINE: By ABBY GOODNOUGH


SECTION: Section B; Page 8; Column 6; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 580 words


As other elected officials criticized the killing of an unarmed immigrant by four police officers, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani defended the Police Department yesterday with a fresh batch of statistics.

Mr. Giuliani used graphs to show that city police officers fired 856 shots in 1998, down from 1,040 shots in 1997. The number of fatal shootings by police officers last year -- 19 -- was the lowest since 1985, a "remarkable" fact given that arrests have risen dramatically, he said.

The statistics were part of the Mayor's Management Report, a self-evaluation that the City Charter requires to be published every six months. Its presentation came as public protest persisted about the fatal shooting of the West African immigrant, Amadou Diallo.

Although Mr. Giuliani said little yesterday about the shooting, he spent an unusual amount of time on police statistics before moving on to other parts of the report.

The Mayor said that the police in Boston, Washington and Chicago fire their weapons more frequently than officers in New York.

"One of the impressions that's given by those who have a hard time giving the police credit for anything is that safety in the city is brought about by the Police Department becoming more violent," he said. "Just the opposite is the case."

Not all the news about the Police Department was good: the police are taking longer to respond to calls about crimes in progress, especially in Brooklyn, the report said. The average citywide response time was 10.5 minutes, up from 10 minutes in 1997. In Brooklyn, the average response time rose to 10.9 minutes, from 10.1 minutes.

"This is an area where the Police Department has to do more work, to find out why, and how that can be reduced," Mr. Giuliani said.

The report, prepared by Mr. Giuliani's staff, trumpets an increase in city bus ridership, a decrease in potholes, an increase in graffiti-related arrests and a decrease in reported cases of tuberculosis.

Only 107 civilians died in fires last year, the smallest number in 52 years, according to the report. From July through October, the average response time for the Fire Department was 4 minutes and 16 seconds, down from 4 minutes and 20 seconds for those same months in 1997.

On a less auspicious note, the city paid its employees $205 million in overtime from July through October, compared with $170 million during that period in 1997. Caseloads for probation officers increased to an average of 239, from 209. And the number of children waiting for subsidized day care slots increased to 13,261, from 12,031.

"It's important not to lose sight of the fact that there still remains plenty of work to be done," said Peter F. Vallone, the Speaker of the City Council.

The report suggested that Mr. Giuliani's civility campaign had not necessarily rubbed off on taxi drivers, who were the target of one of his quality-of-life crackdowns last year.

The Taxi and Limousine Commission received 4,247 complaints about drivers from July through October, up from 3,608 during those months in 1997. Complaints almost doubled in the category of "abusive behavior by driver," to 643 from 319. Complaints in the category of "physical abuse by driver" also increased, as did complaints about traffic infractions.

Mr. Giuliani said the increase in complaints might be due to his highly publicized crackdown on reckless cabbies, suggesting that it had made riders more aware that cabdrivers must be held accountable.

The New York Times


February 11, 1999, Thursday, Late Edition - Final


A Reopened Divide;
Lack of Ties With Black Leaders Hinders Mayor After Police Killing


BYLINE: By DAN BARRY


SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 1306 words


If the greatest accomplishment of the Giuliani administration is the precipitous drop in the city's crime rate, its most glaring shortcoming may be the failure to cultivate a trusting relationship with most of the city's black leaders. Now, the best and the worst have melded into the Diallo case, presenting Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani again with a delicate matter of race relations.

Anger persists over last week's shooting death of Amadou Diallo, an unarmed African immigrant, by four white police officers who riddled his body and apartment-building vestibule with 41 bullets. On Monday, the president of the N.A.A.C.P. attracted national notice by condemning the shooting. On Tuesday, more than 1,000 people staged a heated demonstration in lower Manhattan, with many suggesting that Mr. Giuliani's crackdown on crime had made targets out of all young black men.

And yesterday, Mr. Diallo's mother heeded the advice of the Rev. Al Sharpton and curtly rejected any assistance from the Mayor while in the city to retrieve her son's body.

With every passing day, Mr. Giuliani struggles to bring a calmer perspective to the tragedy, to convey convincingly the pain he says he feels over this loss of life and compassion for the victim's family. Several times he has imparted heartfelt thoughts, then felt the need to defensively recite statistics showing that fatal police shootings are less frequent in New York than in other large cities. His recitation of statistics seems to linger longer than his words of sympathy.

There are several reasons for the Mayor's predicament, including his decision to reserve most comment during the investigation of the shooting. But the overriding factor, critics say, is rooted in the earliest days of Mr. Giuliani's tenure: unlike his predecessors, the Mayor never established a lasting rapport with many of the city's black leaders, either personally or through emissaries. That failure, coupled with various slights and arguments along the way, now leaves him with no capital of trust from which to draw.

That was the clear message at a news conference convened yesterday by C. Virginia Fields, the Manhattan Borough President, and attended by, among others, former Mayor Edward I. Koch, who had troubles of his own with many black political and civic leaders. But rather than score the Mayor, most of the speakers pleaded with him to open the lines of communication.

Being Mayor "requires a willingness to hear," Mr. Koch said.

"So we're saying to the Mayor: 'Listen.' "

Ms. Fields agreed. "I certainly am not blaming Mayor Giuliani or Commissioner Safir for the tragedy that took place," she said, referring to Police Commissioner Howard Safir. Nevertheless, she said that the city "must change the tone and move in a different direction."

Mr. Giuliani responded last night by impeaching the event's credibility, noting that Mr. Koch is a persistent critic and saying that Ms. Fields failed to acknowledge the Police Department's accomplishments, including reduced crime in black neighborhoods. He maintained that he has working relationships with other black leaders -- citing Congressman Charles B. Rangel and former Congressman Floyd Flake -- and tends not to deal with "highly partisan Democrats" who he said strive for headlines rather than for substance. "If you hold a press conference and attack me, attack the Police Department and completely discredit everything that's been done, and if you have somebody who is a persistent critic sitting next to you," the Mayor said, it suggests "no real willingness to work out a real solution. That happens in politics."

Earlier, the Mayor said that he understood the frustration. "I can understand the anger," he said. "I can understand all of it and I feel terrible that this happened as I have expressed on numerous occasions." But he said "that there's a tendency of some people in our society to blame the police in broad strokes that is just as vicious a prejudice as any other prejudice."

Of course, from a purely political perspective, the Mayor -- who harbors hopes of seeking higher office -- may take comfort in remembering that the electorate has twice validated his management style. In fact, he received more votes from black residents in 1997 than he did when first elected in 1993.

Discussing Mr. Giuliani's relations with minority communities, his aides rattle off a series of accomplishments -- from crime reduction to job growth -- that they say have improved the lives of everyone in the city, and offered a familiar and short roster of ministers, business owners and neighborhood leaders who are both black and willing to talk of their admiration for the Mayor.

But this gambit backfired. One of them, the Rev. Michel Faulkner, said yesterday that he met with Mr. Giuliani this week to convey the emotion that the Diallo case has sparked in black neighborhoods, and to suggest that the administration more emphatically display empathy for communities jarred by the shooting. But the minister said he left that meeting feeling frustrated.

"In terms of where we go from here, I really would hope the Mayor would understand the amount of tremendous pain that communities of color are in over this incident," Mr. Faulkner said. "If he does not, I really think this will be a permanent blight on his administration."

From the moment he took office in 1994, Mr. Giuliani's relationship with various black leaders has been complicated, even dysfunctional..

One of the Mayor's first actions was to end the practice of his predecessors of having liaisons to the many constituencies in the city, from gay men and lesbians to Hispanics and blacks. "He didn't want to balkanize the city," said his press secretary, Colleen Roche. State Senator David Paterson and City Councilman Bill Perkins, among others, contend that the decision, in fact, balkanized neighborhoods like Harlem, leaving them with no access to City Hall.

At the center of the tension is the Police Department, whose members have given the Mayor something to brag about -- crime reduction -- even as they resent him for blocking their requests for pay increases. There is the perception that the department's get-tough policy has been largely directed at young black men.

"But the very ones who are being singled out are the ones who are being saved" by the crackdown on crime," said Kenneth T. Jackson, a professor of history at Columbia University. "If you look at just who's alive and who's dead, it's saving the lives of black men. The people who were getting killed were never white women or old white guys or even black women."

"It's a very complicated issue," he added. "It's a terrible situation when people fear government."

Symbolizing that fear of government was the Louima case of 1997, in which a Haitian immigrant was brutalized inside the station house of a Brooklyn precinct. Mr. Giuliani immediately called for a task force to explore community-police relations. But months after his re-election, he ridiculed some of that task force's recommendations with sarcasm that overshadowed the fact that he had set aside $15 million for sensitivity training for officers.

That sarcasm, along with memories of the administration's aggressive handling of the Million Youth March in Harlem last summer, returned to haunt Mr. Giuliani when Mr. Diallo, who was unarmed, was killed. His private meetings with African emissaries, his offers of assistance to the Diallo family, his words of solace -- even his statistical representation of having improved black neighborhoods as much as any other in the city -- wind up appearing to some as insincere somehow.

The Mayor's challenge, according to Dr. James Forbes, the pastor of Riverside Church in Harlem, is to better convey a universal message: "Guess what -- you all count."

Slain Man's Mother Rejects Mayor's Aid


BYLINE: By BLAINE HARDEN


SECTION: Section B; Page 8; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 1233 words


The mother of an African street peddler, whose killing by four police officers has led to angry protests over the human cost of aggressive police behavior, has refused an offer of financial help from Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani.

The Rev. Al Sharpton said yesterday that Kadiadou Diallo, whose son Amadou was killed last week, while unarmed, in a barrage of 41 bullets in the vestibule of his Bronx apartment building, declined the Mayor's offer because she and her husband preferred to accept aid from relatives and other African immigrants.

"I hate to blow the Mayor's bubble here, but they are not preoccupied with the Mayor," said Mr. Sharpton, who has taken a highly visible role in protests over the shooting. "They are preoccupied with how they are going to deal with political injustice and how they are going to bury someone who shouldn't be being buried."

While the victim's mother turned down the city's help in transporting her son's body home to Guinea, a number of prominent current and former city officials criticized the Mayor and the Police Department for what they termed an inadequate and insensitive response to a shooting that remains unexplained. The four police officers, part of an elite street crime unit that patrols at night, have not yet been questioned.

The Manhattan Borough President, C. Virginia Fields, convened a news conference that was attended by Edward I. Koch, the former Mayor, and several other community leaders. Calling for a change of tone by the Mayor and the Police Department, Ms. Fields said city officials should create an atmosphere of respect after explosive incidents like the Diallo shooting.

In an interview, David N. Dinkins, Mr. Giuliani's predecessor as Mayor, said the Mayor's reaction to the shooting was "woefully inadequate."

"This business of, the police always are entitled to the benefit of the doubt, should not operate in every circumstance," Mr. Dinkins said. "Clearly, the facts of this put the burden on the cops to come forward and explain. The Mayor should be asking that question."

In Washington, Kweisi Mfume, president of the N.A.A.C.P., joined those who said the Federal Government had been too passive in letting the Bronx District Attorney direct the investigation. Mr. Mfume spoke of his "utter dismay" at the Justice Department's decision to monitor the investigation, rather than initiate one of its own.

The Mayor told reporters yesterday that while he understood the anger and frustration in the city and felt "terrible" about the shooting, he felt that it would not be fair to draw any "systemic conclusions" from the incident. He also said that "there is a tendency of some people in our society to blame the police in broad strokes that is just as vicious a prejudice as any other prejudice."

Saikou Diallo, the victim's father, said last night after arriving at Kennedy International Airport from Hong Kong that he hoped the four policemen who shot his son would be arrested and "judged fairly."

"I hope, yes, to get justice in America," said Mr. Diallo, who was in Vietnam on business when his son was killed.

The Mayor's office sent people to pick up Mr. Diallo at the airport, but he chose to leave there with Mr. Sharpton and Kyle Watters, a lawyer representing the family. Later, after stopping at a friend's apartment, Mr. Diallo visited the building at 1157 Wheeler Avenue where his son was killed, and spent about 10 minutes inside.

Early yesterday, Mrs. Diallo moved out of the Stanhope Hotel, where the city had offered to pay her hotel bill, and checked into the Rihga Royal Hotel in midtown.

The four officers involved in the 12:45 A.M. shooting last Thursday are Edward McMellon, 26, Sean Carroll, 35, Kenneth Boss, 27, and Richard Murphy, 26.

Soon after the shooting, Police Commissioner Howard Safir ordered the commander of the street crime unit to have daily discussions with his officers about the use of firearms.

"What we are doing is taking a close look at our training procedures, both tactical procedures and ways police officers communicate among each other in enforcement situations," Patrick E. Kelleher, first deputy commissioner, said at a news conference. Mr. Kelleher said, however, that he did not think there was a problem in the way the department handled encounters with suspects.

Mr. Safir has praised the street crime unit, which patrols at night looking for crimes about to occur, as a major reason for a sharp reduction of shootings. But prosecutors have said the unit's aggressive tactics, including searches and chases without probable cause, have sometimes led judges to throw out cases.

While the Mayor canceled a political trip to Texas this week to deal with the shooting, Commissioner Safir traveled on Saturday with his wife to a law enforcement conference in Los Angeles. Asked in Los Angeles yesterday why he left the city in the aftermath of the shooting, Commissioner Safir said only, "I'm going back this afternoon." The Police Department said yesterday that the Commissioner had been in "constant contact" from the time he left.

The Mayor said he saw nothing wrong with the Police Commissioner's attending the conference. "Frankly, you know, there is no crisis or emergency going on and things have been proceeding quite normally," Mr. Giuliani said.

Investigators have not found any witnesses other than the four officers who can explain why the police confronted Mr. Diallo in the doorway of his apartment building or why they fired. The officers' lawyer has said that they flashed badges and that Mr. Diallo, instead of responding to their commands, made gestures they mistook for pulling a gun.

Crime scene investigators, however, have found some evidence that might help explain why the officers, armed with 9-millimeter semiautomatic handguns, fired so many bullets, a police official said yesterday.

The official, who spoke on the condition of anonymity, said investigators found that at least three of the bullets ended up on the street and sidewalk behind the officers, suggesting that they had ricocheted off the door or walls of the building. Perhaps, the official said, the officers had the impression during the fusillade that they were being fired at. "It does not explain why they fired, but it might explain why there was an additional number of rounds," the official said.

Investigators are also exploring what role, if any, Officer McMellon's fall outside Mr. Diallo's building may have played in the shooting. Officer McMellon somehow fell backward, suffering a slight fracture to his tailbone. But investigators say they have yet to learn if the fall created a false impression among the other officers that he had been shot.

Most of the police shell casings were found on the stairs and sidewalk in front of the building, at 1157 Wheeler Avenue. One casing, though, was found in the vestibule, not far from Mr. Diallo's body, investigators said. But officials said that did not necessarily mean that any shots had been fired at close range. The Medical Examiner's office declined comment on whether Mr. Diallo's body showed powder burns, which could indicate a shooting at close range.

After the shooting, the officers did not tell any commanders at the scene what happened, according to investigators, who have interviewed at least 12 officers who arrived at the scene minutes after the shooting.

To Shoot or Not? Fellow Officers Say They Fear Facing Same Decision


BYLINE: By KATHERINE E. FINKELSTEIN


SECTION: Section B;Page 8;Column 1;Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 850 words


The fatal police shooting of Amadou Diallo has provoked many emotions among New York City's officers. Some who spoke yesterday expressed pity for Mr. Diallo and his family. Others said they were surprised by the number of bullets fired and concerned over what the political fallout will be.

But most of all, they said they identified with the four officers who fired 41 shots at Mr. Diallo, a 22-year-old street vendor from Guinea, as he stood in the early hours of Feb. 4 in front of his two-story apartment building in the Bronx with a pager and some keys.

"At night, you see a black shadow coming up, and you might think it's a gun," said Officer Howard Kurz, who was standing outside the Midtown North Precinct station house on West 54th Street yesterday. "I've been a cop for 19 years, worked all over the city, and I'd never arm-chair quarterback what happens at 1 in the morning on a Bronx street."

In interviews with 17 officers in precincts from Harlem to Brooklyn, officers said that they could imagine themselves right there in that vestibule, making a mistake that could cost them their jobs or lead to the death of an innocent civilian.

"I feel sorry for them," said Sgt. Robert Reehil, a plainclothes officer also at Midtown North. "Every day, their names are in the paper. Their addresses are in the paper. Their careers are in the paper." And every day on television, he said, there is file footage of the officers walking out of their lawyer's office.

When he heard the news, Officer Kurz said, he thought: "Those guys are in for a bad ride. They're in the Bronx. They're white cops. Their families are going to be torn up."

The officers who were interviewed yesterday struggled to describe the uncertain world they confront at the beginning of each shift, and the self-doubt that lingers long after the shift ends.

One officer spoke of beginning to squeeze the trigger when he mistook a portable music player for a gun. Another spoke of her fear about whether the commands "freeze" or "don't move" were even comprehensible to many people on the city streets, given the array of languages spoken there.

Then there are the questions that come from officers' spouses, parents and children. Sergeant Reehil said that after the shooting, his wife asked, "How could someone shoot 41 rounds at one guy?" Standing outside the station house yesterday, dressed in khakis and a sweatshirt, he shrugged. "I can't explain it. I wasn't there." But he said his views have been shaped by the fact that his brother, also a police officer, had been shot and wounded in the line of duty.

Yvette Matthews, also an officer at Midtown North, agreed with Sergeant Reehil. "They ask us questions, as though we're supposed to know the answers," he said.

Officers said any of the 30 to 40 calls on a typical shift could explode into catastrophe. Such rapid-fire crises can leave officers stricken by headaches, taut with fear.

"You ever see those kaleidoscopes when you were a kid?" asked Officer Ronald Fagan at the 84th Precinct station house on Tillary Street in Brooklyn. "That's what happens in your brain after a shift and everything you saw and everything you didn't do flashes through your mind."

And then there are the career-ending mistakes.

Some white officers at the 84th Precinct in Brooklyn said they often feel isolated and up against a wall as politicians exploit the racial aspect of such cases and the news media pile on.

"It's more or less like we're political pawns for the public and politicians," said one officer there with 17 years experience, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "In Nassau and Suffolk, they back you up. Here, you're suspended for 30 days before you even go to trial."

A minority officer at the precinct, who joined the conversation, did not question the role of politicians but rather the number of bullets fired.

Officer Fagan said that he thought the police brass were tough on members of the force. "I don't think that anyone will deny that police officers have a profound lack of due process," he said.

Given the treacherous waters they navigate, some officers said that they feel that vigilance, even fear, can be their closest ally. "Fear is survival," said Officer Matthews of Midtown North.

But it can also lead to rash actions -- or no action at all. Officer Kurz recalled the time he was at the Lefrak City apartment complex in Queens and shots were being fired at him. He stood there, terrified, and didn't even take out his gun, he said, because he knew that the shots could have been coming from any one of "10,000 windows."

The reflections of these officers came the day after Mr. Diallo's mother arrived from Guinea to reclaim her son's body and swooned before the house where he was killed. There was little disagreement among officers that the shooting was a tragedy -- a life lost, four careers potentially ended and a police force placed under public scrutiny.

"Whether they were right or wrong," Sergeant Reehil said, "they killed an unarmed man and they have to live with that for the rest of their lives."

February 10, 1999, Wednesday, Late Edition - Final


Giuliani Cancels Political Trip Amid Protest Over Shooting


BYLINE: By ELISABETH BUMILLER with GINGER THOMPSON


SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 1176 words


As more than 1,000 people gathered yesterday in the second angry demonstration in three days to protest the killing of an unarmed African street vendor by four police officers, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani canceled a political trip to Texas so he could meet with the victim's parents and perhaps attend a memorial service on Friday.

The Mayor's announcement came as the mother of the victim arrived in New York from Guinea and visited the Bronx building where her son, Amadou Diallo, 22, died in a barrage of 41 bullets at 12:45 A.M. last Thursday, for reasons still unexplained. Protesters have denounced the shooting and called for the Federal Government to take over the inquiry, but there were indications yesterday that Federal prosecutors would defer to the Bronx District Attorney for now.

Yesterday afternoon, Mr. Diallo's mother, Kadiadou, stepped out of a dark police van at the site of the shooting, fell to the street, was helped to her feet, then cried out "Amadou, Amadou." She was lifted by both arms up the stairs to the building's vestibule, which was filled with flowers and candles and pockmarked with bullet holes.

When she emerged half an hour later from the building at 1157 Wheeler Avenue, she said softly in English that "justice must be done" and that she wanted to take her son and bury him at home.

The demonstration over the killing took place at lunchtime outside the Federal Courthouse in Foley Square, where protesters chanted for more than two hours, denouncing the Giuliani administration and demanding that Federal officials take charge of the investigation into Mr. Diallo's death.

"If they can shoot anyone 41 times," said the Rev. Al Sharpton, who led the protest, "they can shoot everyone 41 times. It may start with blacks and Latinos, but it can go everywhere."

Although many in the crowd were apparently veterans of similar protests, others said they were demonstrating for the first time.

"I take some of the responsibility for what happened," said Roger Bobb, a 31-year-old lawyer from Crown Heights, who said he had never participated in a protest. "I've sat back too long while this kind of thing happens time and time again. I feel that as black people, we have not done enough to show Mayor Giuliani that we will not tolerate this."

While Mr. Giuliani has expressed concern and sympathy about Mr. Diallo's death, he continued to deny yesterday that the shooting was any indication of a pattern of excessive force by the Police Department.

"The use of force by the department, the use of weapons is down, over the last four or five years, dramatically," he said, although accusations against the police for misconduct have risen sharply in New York in the past four years.

Mr. Diallo's death has become a major issue for the administration as it draws national attention to what critics contend is the dark side of Mr. Giuliani's achievements in bringing down crime -- the sense that aggressive police enforcement has created a climate that can breed brutality.

Mr. Giuliani's trip to Texas would have been one of the most prominent in a series of political trips he has been making around the country as a potential candidate for higher office. He was to have made a speech on Thursday in Dallas and then fly to Austin to stay overnight at the Governor's Mansion with Gov. George W. Bush, a leading contender for the Republican Presidential nomination in 2000. Mr. Giuliani and Mr. Bush had plans for a joint news conference on Friday. Aides to the Mayor said that the trip would be rescheduled.

Mr. Giuliani said yesterday at City Hall that a final decision by a grand jury on whether the officers' actions were criminal is likely to take weeks.

"It doesn't make sense for me to comment on it every day because I'm not conducting the investigation," he said.

Police officials have been unable to say why the four white officers thought their lives were in jeopardy or why they fired so many times at Mr. Diallo, who came to America more than two years ago from Guinea and had no criminal record.

The testimony of the four police officers is important because they are apparently the only witnesses to the encounter. They are all members of an aggressive street crimes unit, and three of them have been involved in previous shootings. The officers believed that Mr. Diallo had a gun, their lawyer has said.

The Police Department has not yet interviewed the officers because police officials are deferring to the Bronx District Attorney. The four have also not been questioned by the District Attorney's investigators, because their lawyer has been negotiating the terms under which they would appear before a grand jury.

A police official said that detectives have yet to find other witnesses who saw what happened at the outset of the incident, when the police officers first encountered Mr. Diallo in the vestibule. But one man, Francis Riva, 38, who lives across Wheeler Avenue from the shooting scene, said he told investigators on Monday that he heard three shots at first. "There was a pause between shots," he said. "Then it sounded like they all started firing at once."

The officers were patrolling the neighborhood in an unmarked car looking for a serial rapist.

One of the four officers, Edward McMellon, fell during the encounter with Mr. Diallo, injuring his tailbone. But investigators said they had yet to determine whether that fall played any role in the incident, such as giving his fellow officers the false impression that he had been hit by a gunshot.

Yesterday at the protest, Mr. Diallo's uncle, Mamadou Diallo, spoke to the crowd. "I love my nephew deeply, and I will miss him daily," he said. "And my sadness is made greater knowing that the four people who took his life are still walking free."

The crowd's demand that the case be taken over by Federal investigators will apparently not be met, at least for now. Yesterday the lawyer for the four officers, Stephen Worth, said that lawyers in the United States Attorney's office had assured him that they would monitor the case but not take it from the Bronx District Attorney, Robert T. Johnson.

In return, Mr. Worth said his clients would "most likely" testify before a state grand jury, which is expected to begin hearing evidence later this week.

Yesterday's rally was similar to one held on Sunday in front of Mr. Diallo's apartment building, and Mr. Sharpton said there would be another one next week in front of the Bronx Courthouse.

The crowd at the protest was largely African-Americans and African immigrants. They shouted for justice and pumped their fists in the air. Many said that Mr. Diallo was shot only because of the color of his skin. Others shouted obscenities at the police officers gathered around the perimeter of the rally.

One man, Leslie Stewart, a 50-year-old employee of Bell Atlantic, said that Mr. Giuliani should take responsibility for the shooting.

"If he can't control his employees, he should quit," Mr. Stewart said. "It's clear he cannot control this beast."

Some Favor Federal Role In Police Shooting Inquiry


BYLINE: By BENJAMIN WEISER


SECTION: Section B; Page 6; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 872 words


Behind the many calls for Federal involvement in the police shooting of a Bronx immigrant last Thursday is the concern that state prosecutors may have a tough time bringing a case against the officers.

There is some history that may help to explain the concerns: in 1996, the Bronx District Attorney's office failed to win a conviction in a major police brutality prosecution, when Francis X. Livoti was acquitted of criminally negligent homicide in the death of Anthony Baez. Mr. Baez died in 1994 after a confrontation with the officer that began over a street football game.

After that state acquittal, Federal prosecutors opened a Federal civil rights investigation; Mr. Livoti was convicted and sentenced to seven and a half years in prison.

Any state attempt to prosecute the officers for last week's shooting could be also fraught with legal complications and challenges. Under state law, police officers can use deadly force if they reasonably believe they face the threat or imminent threat of deadly force themselves. In the case last week, the lawyers for the police have argued that the officers had reason to believe that the victim, Amadou Diallo, might have been pulling a weapon on them. He was killed in a barrage of 41 bullets.

"They shot him, The killed him -- they feel very bad about that," Stephen Worth, their lawyer, said of the officers. "That doesn't mean they committed a crime."

In several cases involving officers who said they felt they were in danger and shot unarmed civilians, state prosecutors failed to bring charges.

On Christmas Day in 1997, for instance, Officer Michael J. Davitt shot an unarmed man, William J. Whitfield, in a confrontation at a Canarsie, Brooklyn, grocery store. Police officials said that the officers on the scene believed that Mr. Whitfield was behaving suspiciously, ordered him to halt and then, guns drawn, followed him when he dashed into a grocery store and seemed to hide.

They said Officer Davitt fired the fatal shot when Mr. Whitfield suddenly emerged, rising from a crouch. A state grand jury refused to bring charges, finding the shooting justified under the laws governing use of force.

Federal prosecutors looked into the case, but did not bring charges. Michael A. Hardy, a lawyer for the Whitfield family, said that although no Federal charges were filed, the review had been useful. "One thing about the Federal Government is that they don't have the conflict of interest with the New York City police," Mr. Hardy said. "They have demonstrated some commitment to bringing police up on charges and prosecuting them if necessary."

Federal prosecutors often have more leeway in pursuing civil rights charges in such shootings. Federal rules of evidence are more liberal in some cases than in the state courts. Under the Federal civil rights laws, the Government does not have to prove that an officer caused a victim's death, only that the officer's acts resulted in bodily injury.

One argument for having Federal officials prosecute last week's shooting is that state prosecutors are not as well equipped to handle such cases. During the Federal trial of Mr. Livoti, Federal prosecutors raised some questions about how the Bronx District Attorney's office handled some witness interviews: for instance, when it allowed police officers to meet in a group and discuss the case.

But Norman Siegel, the executive director of the New York Civil Liberties Union, who is a former member of Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's commission on police-citizen relations, said yesterday that many critics were underestimating Mr. Johnson's office.

He cited the 1997 conviction of Paolo Colecchia, who was found guilty of second-degree manslaughter in the fatal shooting of a fleeing unarmed man on a Bronx subway platform in 1996.

"The Johnson lawyers up there successfully prosecuted this cop," said Mr. Siegel, whose organization represented the victim's family. "What they didn't successfully prosecute was Livoti. So the Livoti case sticks in everyone's mind."

The lawyer who defended Mr. Livoti, Stuart London, is working with Mr. Worth on behalf of the officers in the current investigation. Mr. London said that he believed that the fairest result would be for the grand jury investigating Mr. Diallo's death to conclude that the four officers reasonably believed that they were in fear for their lives, based on the suspicious behavior they perceived, and to spare them from criminal charges.

Instead, he suggested, the grand jury could issue a report questioning more generally the police tactics in the case, and suggest procedures that could be followed in the future. "That would be a very viable and constructive option," he contended.

Federal officials have already taken an active role in the latest inquiry. When the Bronx District Attorney's office began interviewing potential witnesses this week, the Federal Bureau of Investigation was there.

But there was a problem that suggested the jockeying that is likely to continue throughout the investigation. Several police officers who were to be interviewed objected to the F.B.I.'s presence, and the officers started talking only when assured that Federal agents would not listen in.

A Mother Arrives From Africa to Mourn Her Slain Son


BYLINE: By KATHERINE E. FINKELSTEIN


SECTION: Section B;Page 6;Column 1;Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 310 words


Within hours of arriving from her homeland, Guinea, yesterday, the mother of Amadou Diallo stepped from a dark police van a few yards from where her unarmed son was killed last Thursday, and instantly crumpled to the street. "Wait, wait, wait," she moaned, clutching her stomach as though in pain, while police officers held back a crowd of reporters and grieving and angry bystanders.

Kadiadou Diallo cried, "Amadou, Amadou," and was half-carried by police officials in business suits and Guinean diplomats in traditional robes into the modest apartment house at 1157 Wheeler Avenue in the Bronx where the police had fired 41 times at her son. The bullet holes riddling the doorframe and hallway had been marked and numbered with a black pen.

Mrs. Diallo, wearing a blue raincoat and with a pale green scarf draped over her head, wailed as she was escorted up the steps where a spray of blood was still faintly visible. "I'm hurt," she said. "I lost my son -- now he's gone." Mr. Diallo's father, a businessman, is expected to arrive today or tomorrow, and the family will then meet with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani.

Once Mrs. Diallo entered the vestibule, which was heaped with flowers and votive candles placed there by neighbors, her wails turned to screams that were audible from the street. She was followed inside by a representative from the Senegalese Embassy, Abid Denj; the President of the Guinean Association of America, Mohamed Jalloh, and several of Amadou Diallo's friends and roommates.

About 45 minutes later, Mrs. Diallo emerged and, speaking softly in careful English, said, "I want to bring him home to bury him." Her son was from a "good family," she added. Just before climbing back into the police van, she was asked by a reporter whether she had a message for New Yorkers. She did.

"Justice," she said, " must be done."

February 9, 1999, Tuesday, Late Edition - Final


Holding Fire


SECTION: Section A; Page 22; Column 1; Editorial Desk 


LENGTH: 436 words


In the movies, a fusillade of 41 bullets is nothing, a Schwarzenegger second. In real life, the storm of police gunfire that killed an unarmed West African immigrant last week was a brutal human tragedy that has left a Bronx neighborhood shaken and angry about the aggressive Street Crimes Unit that is supposed to be making the community a safer place to live.

The shooting occurred shortly after midnight last Thursday when four plainclothes officers from the Street Crimes Unit saw a man in the vestibule of a Bronx apartment building. For reasons that are still unclear, they suddenly emptied their guns at Amadou Diallo, a 22-year-old street merchant from Guinea. Mr. Diallo, who spoke halting English and had no police record, died at the scene.

A lawyer for the policemen told journalists that "fair-minded people" would recognize that "this shooting, although tragic, was proper." He said that the four had been seeking a serial rapist who resembled Mr. Diallo, and that they thought he was acting suspiciously and was reaching for a weapon. They found only a wallet and a beeper in his pockets.

The United States Attorney's office is reviewing the case, which is under investigation by the Bronx District Attorney. One police official told The Times that "it looks as though one guy may have panicked and the rest followed suit." What makes that disturbing is that while the city says that more than 90 percent of New York's police officers never fire their guns, three of the four officers in this case have been involved in shootings before, with two found to have acted properly and one incident still under review by the Brooklyn District Attorney.

Mayor Rudolph Giuliani has wisely counseled New Yorkers not to rush to judgment, and he has offered aid to Mr. Diallo's family through the city's Community Assistance Unit. The Mayor has also assured African diplomats including the Guinean Ambassador that the case is being investigated properly. That is a good start, but the Mayor must find a way to assure the city's immigrants and minorities that policemen from outside their neighborhoods do not reflexively suspect a person of color and resort to force too readily.

Despite efforts by the Mayor and the Police Commissioner to encourage community relations and hold precinct commanders responsible for complaints, there is a long history of suspicion that makes it difficult for many to forgive the fusillade as a simple mistake. It is critically important that city and Federal investigators find answers as quickly as possible to the troubling questions raised by this case.

Inquiry Into Police Shooting Draws the Gaze of Guinea


BYLINE: By KEVIN FLYNN 


SECTION: Section B; Page 4; Column 5; Metropolitan Desk 


LENGTH: 724 words


As friends and family made funeral arrangements yesterday for an unarmed street peddler shot dead by the New York City police, a diplomat from the victim's homeland of Guinea in West Africa said his countrymen were concerned that the four officers involved have not been arrested.

Aboubacar Dione, the first consul of Guinea's permanent mission to the United Nations, said the police shooting of Amadou Diallo, 22, who was killed Thursday in a barrage of 41 bullets, has drawn considerable attention in his country, a nation beset by desperate poverty and convulsed by eight months of civil war.

"People back home -- the entire African continent -- is following this case. They don't understand why these four are still free," Mr. Dione said at the mission on East 39th Street. As he spoke, he received a call from Guinea's minister of foreign affairs, one of many he said he gets about the case each morning from officials in Africa.

While the police have said that there were no witnesses to the shooting, WCBS-TV news broadcast an interview last night with a man who said he lived across the street from Mr. Diallo and had seen part of the incident.

The man, whose identity was not revealed, said he heard shots and went to his window. There was a brief pause, he said, and then the shooting resumed. "By the time I got to the window, now they're shooting again," he said.

The man said that after the shooting stopped, he went down to the street and tried to give information to investigators at the scene, but that he and several other witnesses were told to leave and some were cursed at by the police.

WCBS-TV also said that the man had told his story to the Bronx District Attorney's office, but a spokesman for the District Attorney would not comment last night.

Police officials declined to comment last night on the man's claims.

Yesterday afternoon, the Bronx District Attorney, Robert T. Johnson, met for 30 minutes with six of the dead man's relatives and friends, including his uncle, Mamadou Diallo.

"It was a very good meeting," said Kyle B. Watters, a lawyer for the Diallo family. "The District Attorney assured us he was doing everything he can to see that justice is done. He told us that an investigation was going forward and that they were interviewing potential witnesses today."

Mr. Johnson's office is expected to begin presenting evidence and witnesses to a grand jury later this week. Prosecutors are likely to begin any presentation with a series of witnesses who would establish the broad circumstances of Mr. Diallo's death, such as the fact that he was hit by 19 of the bullets fired at him as he stood unarmed in the vestibule of his Bronx apartment building.

The officers, who were searching for a rape suspect, have not spoken to investigators. But their lawyer, Stephen Worth, has said they thought Mr. Diallo had a gun.

Mr. Worth has said that he would probably allow the officers -- Edward McMellon, 26, Sean Carroll, 35, Kenneth Boss, 27, and Richard Murphy, 26 -- to testify without immunity before a grand jury if certain conditions were met. Three of the officers returned to work on administrative assignments yesterday, and the fourth called in sick, Mr. Worth said.

Mr. Diallo's father and mother are scheduled to arrive in New York today, and the family is planning to hold a memorial service on Friday at the Islamic Cultural Center of New York on East 96th Street. The family is then expected to fly the body of Mr. Diallo home to Guinea, which he left for New York two years ago. Before leaving, the family is expected to meet with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, city officials said.

"No one wants to lose a child," Mr. Giuliani said at a news conference yesterday, "and the idea of even talking to the father and talking to him about his losing his son in a foreign country is a terrible, terrible thing."

Preparations were also being made for a rally at noon today outside United States District Court in Manhattan.

The Mayor spent two hours at City Hall yesterday with members of the United African Congress, his second meeting in as many days with representatives of New York's West Africans. Mr. Giuliani told reporters later that he had tried to explain the slow pace of careful justice. "Investigations, as you know, take longer than anybody expects," he said.

1,000 Rally to Condemn Shooting of Unarmed Man by Police


BYLINE: By GINGER THOMPSON


SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 1170 words


Chanting "no justice, no peace" and saying the time may have come to "fight back," almost 1,000 people staged an angry rally yesterday on a snowy street in front of the Bronx home of a 22-year-old merchant gunned down in a storm of 41 bullets by four New York City police officers.

While community leaders and politicians spoke to the crowd through a microphone muffled by static, the voices and the outrage of the demonstrators -- some of whom had traveled from Connecticut and New Jersey -- came through loud and clear.

Soaked and shivering, they said they had been moved to action by the brutality of the killing last Thursday. The victim, Amadou Diallo, who came to the United States more than two years ago from Guinea, West Africa, was unarmed when he died and had never been in trouble with the law. Like an echo, almost everyone interviewed said the words, "41 bullets," "four officers," and "one man dead." The number of shots, as much as the shots themselves, defied understanding, they said.

And, in a sign that the killing could inflame racial tension in the city, almost everyone interviewed said they believed that the police officers had judged Mr. Diallo as a criminal because of the color of his skin. The only white speaker, Kyle Waters, the lawyer for the relatives of Mr. Diallo, was shouted off the podium.

The crowd stood shoulder to shoulder and nearly filled the block around 1157 Wheeler Avenue, where Mr. Diallo lived and died as police followed him through his front door. It is a worn, but well-kept block of houses and low-rise apartment buildings that neighbors had bragged about as a quiet, peaceful place -- until the shooting.

Yesterday, at least two dozen police officers patrolled the area, posted at the corners of the block and on the subway bridge overhead.

Tensions were high, and most people's voices were filled with contempt. But the rally remained largely peaceful -- with several dozen Muslims offering prayers to Allah and a group of Guineans dancing around a drummer -- except for a flash of tension when half a dozen police officers gathered around the perimeter of the crowd.

Some of the demonstrators rushed over and shouted at the officers. A wall of protesters with calmer heads formed between the angry mob and the police.

"Just let me over there, and I'll kill a police officer," growled one man, trying to push through the line. The police, only a few feet away, glared back.

"Enough is enough!" the crowd of mostly young men began to chant. Behind them, an older man stood wailing: "We're dying. We're dying."

One demonstrator stood in the snow with a bright-yellow banner bearing the words "41 Bullets." He said his first name was Tony and that he was a 31-year-old accountant, but added that he did not think his name was an important identifier. He called himself simply an African-American man -- an identity, he said, that in New York was enough to get you killed by the people who are ordered to protect.

"I am a decent, moral-minded American," he said, looking at the small red-brick apartment building in front of him. "But it seems like that doesn't matter. What happened here, could have happened to me."

The officers who shot Mr. Diallo have not yet spoken with investigators. But their lawyer, Stephen Worth, has said that the officers believed they were in danger because they thought Mr. Diallo had a gun. "They recognize the tragedy that a man died, and was not armed at the time," Mr. Worth said on Saturday.

Most of those in the crowd were African-Americans or immigrants from countries in West Africa, who said they felt as if they had lost a brother. Professionals stood with blue-collar workers who carried homemade posters of skeletons wearing police uniforms. Retirees and college graduates pumped their fists into the air and shouted "Black power!" And parents coaxed their children to pay attention.

The demonstrators demanded that Federal investigators take over the investigation. They attacked Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani as callous for failing to issue a strong condemnation of the crime; for failing to visit Mr. Diallo's relatives and for failing to order the arrest of the officers involved with the shooting.

Mamadou Ka, who came to New York five years ago from Senegal, said of Mr. Giuliani, "When people shoot cops, he is there, but when cops shoot people he doesn't show up."

Making it clear that they do not believe all police officers are bad, the demonstrators expressed deep anger at the officers involved with the shooting. Some people called the officers "renegades," and a "quartet of bandits," and they urged that the officers be immediately arrested and prosecuted for murder.

"I am more afraid of the police than I am of a criminal," said Gloria Jackson, a union organizer, wearing an African scarf over her long, brown braids. "Police look at me and see a black woman, and the first thing they think is that I might cause trouble. They see me as a threat."

The officers' lawyer, Mr. Worth, defended his clients yesterday, saying it was far too early to judge their actions. "In this day and age, it is unfortunate and somewhat predictable that every incident involving the police is used by different groups for their own agenda," Mr. Worth said in a telephone interview. "I hope that all fair-minded people will await the conclusion of the investigation. If they do they will see that the police conduct, although tragic, was justified."

David Bryant, 43, who came to yesterday's rally from Bloomfield, N.J., urged that the New York Police Department assign officers to patrol the neighborhoods they live in. "We have police officers who have never been to a black neighborhood out here," he said. "All they see is a black man and a black face and they see trouble."

Referring to Mr. Diallo, he added, "If the officers had come from this neighborhood, they would have known this man wasn't no criminal."

And Joyce Pinckney, 60, who performs background checks for prospective New York City police officers, said she felt there needed to be more sensitivity training for officers, and stronger messages from the top of the department that abuse of authority would not be tolerated.

The rally, organized by the Rev. Al Sharpton, was attended by the relatives of several people who had been killed in police shootings. And Michael Jones, who was shot 17 times by two police officers in August in Brooklyn, attended the rally in a wheelchair.

"It opened up all the wounds again of all the mothers that are here," said Iris Baez, whose son Anthony was killed in a confrontation with police officers in 1994.

Mr. Sharpton told the crowd he would continue to call on city officials to place the officers involved in the shooting on trial, and he urged them to attend another rally scheduled for Tuesday at Foley Square.

"We are not anti-police," Mr. Sharpton said. "We are anti-police brutality.

"A criminal is a criminal, whether he is wearing a blue uniform or blue jeans," he said.

Police Killing Draws National Notice


BYLINE: By KEVIN FLYNN


SECTION: Section B;Page 5;Column 1;Metropolitan Desk


LENGTH: 1252 words


The outcry over the killing of an unarmed West African immigrant in a fusillade of 41 police bullets spread to the national stage yesterday, as the president of the N.A.A.C.P. condemned the shooting as "excessive force at its worst" and urged the United States Attorney General, Janet Reno, to reassure Americans that such incidents will not be tolerated.

The president, Kweisi Mfume, said he would ask Ms. Reno today to make a public statement that "the Justice Department will use every possible vehicle to get to the facts involved in the case and make sure that justice is fair and swift."

Mr. Mfume's comments were the first indication that national attention is beginning to focus on the death of Amadou Diallo on Thursday. Mr. Diallo, a 22-year-old street peddler with no criminal record, was hit by 19 of the bullets fired by four police officers in the vestibule of his Bronx apartment building.

Mr. Mfume said he did not think it necessary for the Justice Department in Washington to take over the criminal investigation, which is being directed by the Bronx District Attorney's office and being monitored by the United States Attorney's office in Manhattan. But he said Federal officials needed to do more now than just review the incident in anticipation of a possible civil rights case down the road.

"Saying you are monitoring it does not assure Americans enough that there is going to be a quick attempt to get to the facts of the case," he said.

A spokesman for Ms. Reno, Myron Marlin, said, "The Civil Rights Division in Washington is already working together with the local U.S. Attorney's office, but we will consider any request by Mr. Mfume and will determine what additional action will be needed."

Police officials have been unable to explain why the four officers, all members of an aggressive Street Crimes Unit with five to seven years' experience on the force, thought their lives had been in jeopardy, or why they had fired so many times. The officers, who were searching for a rape suspect when they encountered Mr. Diallo, have yet to speak to investigators.

But their lawyer, Stephen Worth, has said the officers had believed Mr. Diallo had a gun. According to a person close to the officers, the four said Mr. Diallo did not respond to their spoken commands and made motions with his hands and arms that the officers interpreted as threatening. When the barrage of bullets had ceased, however, only a wallet and a pager were found next to Mr. Diallo's body in the doorway of 1157 Wheeler Avenue.

A state grand jury is scheduled to begin hearing testimony this week as it evaluates whether the officers should be charged criminally. Mr. Worth has said he will probably allow the officers to testify at that proceeding without immunity as long as he is assured that the Federal authorities will not intervene in the criminal case until the state grand jury has completed its deliberations.

In a statement yesterday, the office of the United States Attorney in Manhattan, Mary Jo White, hinted strongly that it intended to let the state case go forward before deciding whether to proceed with a Federal civil rights case of its own.

"We have complete confidence in the ability of the Bronx District Attorney to determine whether the shooting violated any state laws," Marvin Smilon, a spokesman for the office, said in a statement. "We will coordinate closely with Mr. Johnson's office as his investigation proceeds." He referred to Robert Johnson, the District Attorney.

While the testimony of officers in shooting incidents is always important, the accounts of the officers in this case -- Sean Carroll, 35, Edward McMellon, 26, Kenneth Boss, 27, and Richard Murphy, 26 -- are particularly so because there were apparently no other witnesses. Detectives have been scouring Mr. Diallo's neighborhood, the Soundview section of the Bronx, for witnesses, without success.

Asked to clarify whether Federal prosecutors were committed to letting the state case proceed first, Mr. Smilon said he could not elaborate. But he did offer a more detailed explanation of why Federal prosecutors decided to enter the case so early, saying it was based not on a particular request from anyone, but on "the public importance of the matter and the possibility that Federal civil rights laws might be implicated."

The lawyer for the officers, Mr. Worth, said he would call prosecutors today in hopes of settling terms under which his clients would testify. But he said the Federal authorities, so far, had made no promises to him.

"I'll have to hear from the horse's mouth before making a decision as to whether they will testify," he said.

The officers have been removed from patrol and given administrative assignments pending the investigation, which is routine.

The shooting is one in a series of police incidents that have led some New Yorkers to complain that the same aggressive police work credited in part for the decline in crime has also led to an increase in police brutality, particularly against minority residents. Mr. Diallo was black; the officers are white.

Mr. Mfume alluded to that context while announcing the decision by the National Association for the Advancement of Colored People to join a chorus of voices in New York expressing outrage at the shooting.

"The N.A.A.C.P. will be joining with other concerned citizens in an attempt to underscore the absolute foolishness of excessive police brutality that has reared its ugly head again in New York, and again involving a person of color," he said. "The N.A.A.C.P. simply does not believe there is any legitimate excuse or explanation for what took place."

Mr. Worth said Mr. Mfume was too quick to judge the incident.

"How anyone on the outside can know that this was an unjustifiable shooting mystifies me," he said. "Both the Police Commissioner and the Mayor recognized that the mere number of shots alone does not make this a crime on the part of the police officers."

Mr. Mfume, who spent 10 years in Congress as a Democratic Representative from Maryland, said he also planned to ask Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani to quickly and forcefully criticize the police behavior as he did in the case of Abner Louima, the Haitian immigrant whom police officers have been accused of torturing.

"He was quick to denounce it, and I would like him to use his bully pulpit to let New Yorkers know he denounces this as well," Mr. Mfume said.

Colleen Roche, a spokeswoman for Mr. Giuliani, said the Mayor had already expressed his support for Federal involvement in the investigation and his sympathy about the death of Mr. Diallo, who immigrated from Guinea two years ago.

"Is he concerned about the fact that 41 shots were fired? Absolutely," she said. "But he would like to wait until all the facts are in before making that kind of judgment."

The Mayor spent part of yesterday afternoon in a 45-minute meeting at City Hall, where he briefed the Guinean Ambassador to the United Nations, Mahawa Bangoura Kamara, and the Guinean Ambassador to the United States and Brazil, Mohammad Thiam, on the case.

"They talked about keeping lines of communication open and told them we would apprise them of new facts as they develop," Ms. Roche said.

On Saturday, Ms. Roche said, the Mayor spoke by telephone with Mr. Diallo's father, who is in transit to New York from Vietnam, where he was on business. But Ms. Roche said she could not discuss what the two men had said, calling it a private conversation.

February 7, 1999, Sunday, Late Edition - Final


Fatal Police Barrage Renews Debate Over Safety of Semiautomatics


BYLINE: By JODI WILGOREN 


SECTION: Section 1; Page 43; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk; Second Front


LENGTH: 1394 words


The type of gun used by four New York City police officers last week in the fatal shooting of an unarmed West African immigrant is the weapon of choice among law enforcement agencies across the country in the continually escalating arms race between the police and criminals, experts say.

The barrage of 41 bullets sprayed at 22-year-old Amadou Diallo in the vestibule of his Bronx building early Thursday has revived questions about the safety of 9-millimeter semiautomatic handguns, which fire faster and carry more bullets than the .38-caliber revolvers they replaced in the New York City Police Department six years ago.

Yesterday, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani defended the use of 9-millimeters and praised the New York force for "being just about the most restrained Police Department in the country," even as he described the shooting as a "terrible tragedy."

Experts say that there is no clear link between semiautomatics and the number of shootings by the police, and that officers are likely to get higher-powered weapons rather than go back to traditional handguns.

More than 99 percent of the nation's municipal police forces with more than 100 officers now authorize the use of semiautomatic weapons, with 74 percent of the agencies allowing 9-millimeter handguns like the ones involved in the New York incident, according to a forthcoming study by the Federal Justice Department.

New York was among the last to switch to semiautomatics; most cities opted for more firepower in the mid-1980's, many prompted by a 1986 shootout in Miami in which F.B.I. agents ran out of bullets.

"The threats nowadays are a lot more than they were years ago," said Robert DelCore of the International Association of Chiefs of Police. "If you go back 30 years, you'd have a guy who might have pulled a gun at you but who wouldn't fire. Go back 20 years, maybe they'd fire at you, a little Saturday Night Special. Now it's escalated to where they carry little machine guns. You've got to be prepared to meet the threat."

Criminologists said there had been no studies comparing the number and severity of police shootings with semiautomatics versus revolvers, and a spokesman for the New York City Police Department said he could not separate statistics based on the type of weapon used. While several experts said anecdotal evidence indicates that the number of shots fired per incident increases when guns such as the 9-millimeter are used, they also noted that the number of police shootings -- in New York and across the nation -- has declined sharply even as the semiautomatic weapons have become standard issue.

In New York, the rate of fatal police shootings per 1,000 officers was 0.48 last year, the lowest since 1985, and lower than the rates in Chicago, Dallas, Miami, Philadelphia or Washington. The number of shootings, and the shots fired per incident, have fallen with the crime rate during the last four years: There were 344 shootings with an average of 5 rounds fired in 1995, and 249 incidents with an average of 3.4 shots fired last year, according to Inspector Michael Collins, an N.Y.P.D. spokesman.

The critical question in analyzing police shootings, law enforcement experts said, is why officers pulled the trigger, not which guns they were using.

"Whatever the gun is, because they can all kill, the bigger problem is the quick trigger," said David Lerner, a spokesman for the Center for Constitutional Rights.

"It's much more about training, accountability and protocol than it is about the weapon," said Paul Chevigny, a law professor at New York University. "I don't want to sound cold-hearted; Mr. Diallo might be alive if they hadn't had automatic weapons. But I don't think it makes that much difference."

As a practical matter, the difference would likely have been in the number of shots fired. Two of the four officers emptied their 16-bullet magazines in the incident: Had they been carrying revolvers, they would have had just 6 bullets each before needing to reload. The Medical Examiner says 19 bullets hit Mr. Diallo.

Another difference is in the impact of the bullets hitting their target. Unlike the .38, experts said, several bullets fired from a 9-millimeter could hit a man without knocking him down, which could lead officers to continue shooting.

"These bullets would be passing through him so fast that he could still be standing," explained Steven M. Edwards, who oversees police research for the Federal Government's National Institute of Justice. "You have to remember this is happening in nanoseconds. There's fright and flight that are operating here as well. It's not something as rational as it may sound."

The police favor the 9-millimeter handguns not only because they discharge quickly, unloading up to 18 bullets in about four seconds, but because they carry more rounds and can be reloaded simply and fast.

As a semiautomatic, the 9-millimeter, like the .38-caliber revolver it replaced, fires only one round each time the trigger is pulled. But it recocks itself after each shot, placing another bullet in the chamber, and thus can release a succession of bullets quickly.

Lighter than the .38, the "9," as it is known, rests more naturally in a shooter's palm, and often increases accuracy. But the real attraction is the magazine, which holds up to three times as many bullets as a revolver, and the ease of reloading. A 9 is refilled in a two-step process that takes about four seconds, can be done in the dark, and has been compared to popping a disk into a computer; reloading a revolver takes two hands, eight separate movements, and about 12 seconds.

New York originally allowed magazines of just 10 bullets, and now uses clips of 16. The city also commissioned a special device that increases the amount of pressure needed to pull the trigger, from about 5.5 pounds to about 12 pounds. Most patrol officers use semiautomatics, though a handful of veterans have kept their .38's, officials said. All four of the officers in the Bronx shootings had 9-millimeters; two were Sig Sauer models, one was a Glock and one was manufactured by Smith & Wesson, the police said yesterday.

In addition to the potential for increased number of rounds fired, critics question the reliability of the 9-millimeters. "In a way, it's like saying Porsches are faster and more powerful than Chevys, so we should equip the cops with Porsches," said James T. Fyfe, a criminologist at Temple University who questioned the shift to nines. "Chevys start every morning."

But the public does not like seeing the police outgunned. Police shootings with large numbers of shots fired cause little outrage compared with incidents like the Los Angeles bank robbery in 1997 in which officers had to borrow weapons from a gun shop to battle suspects with machine guns.

Rather than reconsider the move toward 9's, police departments are looking at even more powerful guns: 15 percent of the municipal departments currently authorize 10-millimeter semiautomatics, according to the upcoming Justice Department study, 41 percent use .45-calibers and 50 percent use .40-calibers, both more powerful guns than the 9-millimeter. Some departments, experts say, are now allowing officers to carry fully automatic rifles in their cars rather than shotguns.

"The lingering concern would be whether there is adequate policy and enforcement and training having to do with how many shots you fire and when to stop shooting," Mr. Edwards said.

Thomas Walsh, vice president of New York's Police Benevolent Association, said the semiautomatic handgun "is a tool of necessity today," and noted that New York police officers are "trained to shoot to stop."

"If an incident is stopped in one round, then we shoot one round," he said. "If it's stopped in five, it'll be that many."

While it remains a mystery why all four officers fired, and fired so many times, in the Bronx shooting, Mr. Fyfe said that, in general, he is more troubled by incidents in which only one officer out of a group fires. "You have to wonder what he saw that no one else saw," Mr. Fyfe said, speaking hypothetically. "If you think about the options here, you either have four homicidal maniacs who happen to be working together, or you have four rational guys who all saw the same thing. The chances are, you don't have four homicidal maniacs."
